Clause 565 (Dividends payable from profits only).
Mr JOHN BROWN (Parramatta) (9.23)- We are now dealing with clause 565, dividends payable from profits only. This clause causes the Opposition some worry. In fact, the Opposition included this clause in our second reading amendment. The clause now only provides that no dividends shall be payable to the shareholder of any company except out of profits. Whilst the clause gives an inclusive definition of dividends it does not give any definition of profit. Sub-clause (2) assumes that the concept of profit is clearly defined and understood for this sub-clause makes it an offence for a director or executive officer of a

company wilfully to pay dividends out of what he knows is not profits. For that reason alone a definition of profits is totally desirable. In the view of the Opposition, more importantly, in failing to define profits the Bill leaves the way open for a liberal interpretation of the concept and for the strictures of this clause to be avoided.
In the exposure draft of the Companies Bill clause 376 defined profits, for the purpose of payment of dividends, as being the sum of:
(a) The aggregate of its revenue profits so far as not previously applied (whether by distribution, capitalisation or otherwise); and
(b) Tbe amount, if any, by which the aggregate of its realised capital profits so far as not so previously applied exceeds the aggregate of revenue losses and realised capital losses so far as not previously written off.
The proposed clause contained further strictures relating to the inclusion of capital profits in the profits against which dividends could be debited. There was some criticism of these provisions from an accounting point of view. Probably some of the criticisms were justified. However, it is a great pity that the Government got cold feet in this area.
Mr J acohi-They watered it down.
Mr JOHN BROWN-That is why they got cold feet, because they used cold water to water it down. What was a most important step towards reform has been abandoned and the interpretation of the concept of profit left entirely to the directors of the company. While we may share some of the views of the Minister that not all directors are thieves, cheats or brigands, I think the Minister would also agree that there are quite a number of directors, in and out of gaol, who perhaps would not qualify for that description. The Opposition is quite concerned that the definition of profits is almost left to the value judgment of a director. The Opposition regrets that instead of persisting with a worthwhile reform and endeavouring to get it right the Government has succumbed to unreasonable pressure from accountants and other lobbyists and that good reform in thc exposure draft, like a lot of other good reforms, has disappeared from the Companies Bill. We regret that that is what has happened. This is another one of the many amendments which we think need to be made to the Companies Bill to make it the Bill that we all want, given the fact that it is a co-operative scheme rather than a uniform scheme. Even with that fact in mind if this amendment were cleared up perhaps there might be more protection.

I want to raise two issues relating to Schedule 9, particularly in regard to clause 2L. I suggest that the Schedule is inadequate in a number of important aspects. I trust that my repeated requests for an accounting standards review board finally will be heeded by the Ministerial Council. Perhaps the Minister might give me some indication at this stage of just how far the Ministerial Council has got in regard to that. In my view, two sub-clauses should be added to clause 2L of Schedule 9. There are a couple of reasons for that. Firstly, turnover sales ought to be disclosed. They are not at the moment. Secondly, total wage and salary costs ought to be disclosed. There is no provision in Schedule 9 for that at the moment. Why is that? One cannot possibly calculate the efficiency of a firm without sales figures. The legislation is defective in that regard. It does not give shareholders the right to make a balanced evaluation for analysis of a company's liquidity. A second point on the matter of wages is that disclosure involves a socially significant payment to the community, to both shareholders and to the public. Obviously it is important for a number of other reasons. It is quite unfortunate that this provision has never appeared in Schedule 9. The other important factor is that employers can gauge distribution to employees, governments and shareholders. This provision is equally important-I can understand the corporate sector not wanting this-in order to determine the wages component in the price structure. I hope the Ministerial Council, whatever it does and whatever guidelines and criteria it fixes, ranks those two matters as important.
It is appalling that such information is not disclosed-not made public-to enable proper evaluation. I go back to what the Minister said before. I have a different point of view. The situation to which I refer occurs in insurance companies in particular. Britain has found it to be much needed and long overdue to have more adequate information in balance sheets, whether they are quarterly, half-yearly or annual balance sheets. We tend to overlook the fact that 15 per cent of company shareholders cannot understand a balance sheet. Of those remaining-the real watchdogs-only 4 per cent or 5 per cent, excluding directors, can understand balance sheets. The failure to provide for appropriate disclosures on balance sheets is one of the real difficulties in effecting the early detection ofliquidity problems. In regard to proper accountancy procedures, I want to raise with the Minister a matter that I

consider to be critically important. It is one that I hope he takes to the Ministerial Council. In my view it should rank extremely highly amongst the criteria fixed ultimately by the review board. It is an issue that ought to be given priority. I ask the Minister to take a brief and refer it to the National Companies and Securities Commission for urgent consideration. Perhaps the Minister will understand and grasp that concept more than most others. I am dealing now with winding up, liquidation, and things such as that. The brief is this: The Companies Act must give some guidance in determining the criteria which should be used for changing from going concern values to liquidation values.
Mr Moore-Yes, I was talking about that.
Mr JACOBI-Exactly. I think it is an appalling defect. I will undertake to put an appropriate question on the Notice Paper, perhaps tomorrow. If we consider the number of companies that have gone into liquidation, this sort of information, this sort of criteria, is critically important.
Mr MOORE (Ryan-Minister for Business and Consumer Affairs) (9.41)- The latter point raised by the honourable member for Hawker (Mr Jacobi) was the point to which I was referring when I responded previously. I have very grave reservations about the practice of making valuations, announcing valuations, on which people hold securities. They can then sell them and come out subsequently, some years later, with a far better result. Certainly the amounts returned by the receiver are substantial.
On the question of accounting procedures and accounting reviews, there is no doubt at all that standards vary enormously. However, I do not accept that the way to do something in this life is to adopt the view: When in doubt, regulate. There is a great old bureaucratic practice which says: 'We do not know anything about it. We will regulate it'. I have seen governments in Queensland and in other places do this. The most appropriate course would be for the National Securities Commission to determine the minimum acceptable to it--not the maximum but the minimum. From that base, discussions could be held with the Institute of Chartered Accountants or the Society of Accountants in which they could recognise what should become the minimum acceptable. By doing that we could save ourselves yet another government body, board or regulatory authority. At the same time, it is hoped that we would see accountants reach those standards. If they do not reach

CROWN DEBTS (PRIORITY) BILL 1981
Second Reading
Debate resumed from 30 April, on motion by MrMoore:
That the Bill be now read a second time.
Mr JOHN BROWN (Parramatta) (9.46)-1 move:
That all words after 'That' be omitted with a view to substituting the following words:
'whilst not opposing the Bill, the House: (1) expresses its concern that Crown Priorities in relation to tax instalment deductions and witholding tax on dividends and interest remitted overseas, and the priority of the Crown in right of the States, have been preserved, and (2) calls upon the Government to(a) abolish all remaining Crown Priorities in right of the Commonwealth and to seek the abolition of all remaining Crown Priorities in the State sphere, and (b) ensure that all wages and compensation payments to employees are given priority over all creditors'.
In June 1978 the Senate Standing Committee on Constitutional and Legal Affairs tabled in the Senate its report entitled 'Priority of Crown Debts'. The report was in response to a reference to the Standing Committee from the Senate on 21 April 1977 to review the right of priority of the Crown over other creditors in matters of bankruptcy, corporate liquidations, and other cases of impecunious persons or corporations. In that report the Senate Standing Committee recommended the complete abrogation of all Crown priorities in insolvency administration. The Federal Government-this Government-went some way along the line. The then Minister for Business and Consumer Affairs, the present Minister for Education (Mr Fife), in a statement to the House of Representatives on 13 September 1979 said that the Government had examined the report and, whilst it was fully in agreement with the main thrust of the Committee's argument, it was not willing to abrogate its total rights to Crown priority.
The Government therefore went to the States with the view that the Crown should retain priority in the areas of tax instalment deductions, withholding tax on undistributed dividends, and interest remitted overseas. When it came time for the Ministerial Council to look at this proposition, the States were unwilling to forgo their rights. Because the Commonwealth was not prepared to forgo all its rights, the States were likewise inclined. We now have the situation mentioned by the honourable member for Henty (Mrs Child)

2346 REPRESENTATIVES 13 May 1981

Crown Debts (Priority) Bill

and the honourable member for Banks (Mr Mountford) in the debate on clause 441 of the Companies Bill, under which employees' rights come way down the line. The Opposition finds this an unacceptable and most reprehensible state of affairs. Firstly, in the case of a failed company, secured creditors get their slice. They are followed by the Commonwealth, then the State, and finally, way down the line, in paragraphs (e), (f) and (g), the employees have an opportunity to get something from what is left of the wreck. We cannot accept that as being a moral state of affairs.
As the honourable member for Henty pointed out, and it has been pointed out in this debate by other speakers, the people who work for companies in fact are investing their labour in the fortunes of that company with just as much sincerity as a shareholder is investing his money. However, the particular characteristic of an employee's efforts is that in almost every case the only chance an employee of a company has of getting anything back is what he gains in wages from that company. The Minister for Business and Consumer Affairs (Mr Moore) suggested some while back that shareholders come even further down the line than employees.
Mr Moore-They do.
Mr JOHN BROWN-Perhaps that might be a case of regret also. It may well be that a case is made out for them to have priority over the Crown. I think that the pertinent point to be remembered here is that most people who are investing in companies, particularly large public companies, do not have all their eggs in one basket. It is a very rare investor who puts all his money into one company. So it is possible that, if a company fails and an investor loses his or her investment in that company, he or she does have some other form of living; but with an employee that is not the case. We are not prepared to accept that salary earners, when accepting their share of what is left, are placed right at the bottom of the priority list. I put it very firmly to the Minister that the Crown Debts (Priority) Bill and section 441 of the Companies Bill should be looked at to see that our view, which was the view of the Senate Standing Committee on Constitutional and Legal Affairs, is accepted and that employees rights are put right at the top of the list of priorities.

employees? In fact, due to their downgraded position right at the bottom of the priorities list, the total possible payment to anyone employee under these circumstances is $2,000. Mr Deputy Speaker, I hope that both you and the Minister believe that the Opposition is very sincere in its endeavours in relation to this Bill. We believe we have a right, as I think the government has a right, to protect the workers of Australia. In the case of failed companies, it is usually not the workers fault that the company fails. Other people might put forward a countervailing argument, but in almost every case of company failure in Australia it could never be that the guilt would be sheeted to the employees. Almost always it has been the fault of the management or perhaps the economy generally that a company has failed, but certainly not the fault of the employees. How in heaven's name could anyone suggest that when a company does fail and employees are put out of work they cannot receive wages owing to them unless they are placed right at the bottom of the priorities list? I hope that the Minister would heed those remarks. I hope that, when the Ministerial Council discusses this Bill, the Opposition's amendment will be listened to and invoked.
Mr DEPUTY SPEAKER (Mr Millar)-Is the amendment seconded?
Mrs CHILD (Henty) (9.53)-Yes, I second the amendment. I do so very strongly. I think that the plight of the employee when the firm by which he is employed goes broke is pretty deplorable. Recently several firms in Melbourne, particularly manufacturing firms, have collapsed and a receiver has been appointed. In each case several hundred employees were immediately out of work, and with little warning. I will return to that aspect later. However, their plight is even worse than that of persons who are sacked or rendered redundant because in those cases people at least receive payment for the weeks they have worked, plus a week's notice with pay. Those men and women who have given their services and fulfilled their contracts with the firm concerned not only did not receive the pay that they were waiting for, which was probably needed to pay the rent and buy groceries, but also did not receive a week's notice pay, long service leave pro rata payments and holiday pay. The employees are way down the line as regards the priorities list.

The Commonwealth can hardly say that it has no other way of obtaining revenue. With all the avenues which are open to government, should it think that it is entitled to have a share above the wages which are due to employees in a failed company or of compensation payments due to

What happens when a firm goes broke, firstly, is that the secured creditors receive first call on the available finance followed closely by the Taxation Office, which we all seem to have a bogy about. It collects the accumulated pay-as-you-earn instalments. In the cases which I outlined this

COMPANIES (ACQUISITION OF SHARES) AMENDMENT BILL (No.2)
1981 Second Reading
Consideration resumed from 30 April, on motion by Mr Moore:

In Committee

Mr JOHN BROWN (Parramatta) (10.9)- On behalf of the Opposition, I move:
That Part III, comprising clauses 10 and 11, be omitted.
These clauses remove the prohibitions on crossings by brokers acting for an offeror either on or off market. The Government indicates in its explanatory memorandum on this Bill, although the Minister for Business and Consumer Affairs (Mr Moore) made no mention of the matter in his second reading speech, the following:
Part III of the Bill will not be proclaimed to come into operation until the Ministerial Council agrees unanimously to do so.
Before deciding to proclaim Part III, the Ministerial Council will consider:
(a) reports on whether stock exchange business rules need changes to deal adequately with conflicts of interest during crossings; and
(b) draft changes to the securities industry code relating to the enforcement of the relevant stock exchange business rules and appropriate disciplinary procedures for dealers.
The Opposition objects to these amendments on two grounds. Firstly, as I mentioned in my speech on the motion that the Companies Bill be read a second time, what the Government is asking this Parliament to do is to pass into law provisions that we do not yet know are desirable or

Companies Amendment Bill (No.2)

13 May 1981 REPRESENTATIVES 2349

feasible. The Government is asking us to abrogate our responsibility and to hand over the decision to a body which is accountable to no one parliament. We consider this to be a breach of parliamentary processes which are all too often treated with contempt by this Government.
Our second objection relates to the fact that we have no confidence that the business rules of the stock exchanges or, indeed, the policing capacity of these exchanges is adequate to deal with conflicts of interest during crossings. For example, we observe that the exchange in Sydney as reported in the Press on 8 May saw fit to fine an unnamed stockbroker $10,000 last week for malfeasance in dealing with a parcel of the dreaded Negri River Corporation Ltd shares. Strangely enough the broker's name has not been revealed.
We figure this to be a weakness in the Companies Bill whereby the stock exchange can take unto itself the regulation of the activities and the behaviour of the brokers within that exchange. A person can be dealing, as this gentleman obviously had, dishonestly with a parcel of shares, can be fined $10,000 by that stock exchange and his name not be revealed to the Press. This gentleman, being a peer of the establishment, a pillar of society or whatever, is nothing more than a common criminal. There is something inherently wrong in this Bill where the stock exchange does not have the capacity, the right or, indeed, the intention of revealing this man's name in public. If he had been a shoplifter pinching a seven and sixpenny toy from Woolworths his name would have been revealed to the Press. But it appears that there is one law for the unwary and one law for the rich.

particularly helpful to the Opposition. The Minist¢r has fully made them available to us. That fact is appreciated by us. We thank them for their co-operation.
Mr MOORE (Ryan~~ Minister for Business and Consumer Affairs) (10.16)-1 would also like to thank those who participated in this debate, particularly the honourable member for Parramatta (Mr John Brown) and the honourable member for Hawker (Mr Jacobi) who very obviously have made a considerable study of what has been in the Bills and have gone out of their way to inform themselves on a matter which does not really entice a lot of people to take much notice of. I commend them for the effort they have put into it and certainly for the knowledge that they have imparted during the debate tonight. It is a very helpful contribution to what I hope will be a fairly memorable occasion insofar as it is the first time in Australia's history that we have had a national companies Bill passed by the national Parliament.
In relation to crossings, 1 hate to inform the honourable member for Parramatta that the man not named by the Stock Exchange was not involved in a crossings problem. Nevertheless, this case does bear out some of the migivings that people have with the Stock Exchange as a whole. What people really need to do is to brush up on their public relations and to see far more of their public responsibilities. I will say something about crossings because this is one of those areas where very few people have an idea of what it is all about. There is plenty of recrimination and plenty of saying: 'It's all wrong'. But there is no great background of understanding what it is about.

We consider that Part III of this Bill which we would like omitted is a very weak arrangement. 1 know that the Minister has varying views from the Opposition on the subject of crossings. Perhaps he will give us the benefit of his wisdom in a moment or two. He cannot deny the inherent weakness of this Bill, in that the self-regulating policies of the stock exchange are like the self-regulating policies of anything else-nobody is competent to judge himself fairly and objectively. I suggest to the Minister that we hope that Part III of this Bill will be eliminated from the total Bill because we consider that it points up inherent weaknesses in the Bill. We consider that this whole area needs redrafting.
Before 1 conclude, since this is the last time that 1 will be speaking for the Opposition on this total great package of Bills, 1 think it would be reasonable to pay some sort of tribute to the Minister's staff sitting up there in the wings. They have been

The term 'crossing' simply means a sale within a broker's office, that is, broker to broker and from client to client. In those circumstances a relationship develops between a client and a broker in exactly the same way as between a patient and a doctor. The most important thing to anyone, after health, is a person's shares or money. 1 think everyone in this place and some of those who have gone will probably recognise that. Because of that the relationships between a broker and his client are particularly close.
I go on to the next stage because most of the problems appear to be in the area of crossings associated with an offer. An offer is made for a company and is well publicised in the Press. Notice of the offer is posted on the boards of the stock exchange. Those brokers who are dealing in a stock not only are presumed to have read the newspapers but also are presumed to have read the notices on the boards within the stock

2350 REPRESENTATIVES 13 May 1981

Companies Amendment Bill (No.2)

exchange. So, on two counts they are adjudged to know of the offer. Under those circumstances a client who is a client of the broker acting for the offeror who wishes to sell his shares then approaches his broker with that order to sell. He is offering his shares for sale in the knowledge of the offer because it is advised in the newspapers. The broker knows about it because he has read about it in the newspapers. If he has not, that notice is on the board on the floor of the Stock Exchange where the deal must take place and, as a consequence, he is informed. Therefore, in my view a crossing within the full knowledge of both the buyer and the seller is quite within the bounds of commercial activity and quite within the rules of the stock exchange.
Mr Jacobi---But the rules of the stock exchange are defective in that case.
Mr MOORE-They are not at all. I am surprised that the honourable member for Hawker seems not to understand that these are transactions taking place in a fully informed market.
Mr John Brown--What about before the offer is made public?
Mr MOORE-The honourable member for Parramatta has raised a point. 1 think I told him about this in the corridor. That problem can only really take place where a broker is building up a holding on behalf of an offeror prior to the offer being made. Under the circumstances of the new Bill dealing with the National Companies and Securities Commission that relates to shares prior to the 20 per cent threshold. Under those circumstances a broker acting for an offeror accumulating up to the 20 per cent mark on a rising market will, of course, be buying shares at a varying price. A client of that broker offering to sell on those price patterns needs to know of these activities or have complete trust in that broker. It is only within that context that J see a problem within the prospect of crossings.
I take the view that most brokers are honest. I take the view that most clients trust the activities of their broker. As I have said before, we are dealing with a person's most precious commodity-his money. Therefore, I say that it would be a very silly broker who acted not with the best interests of his client at heart despite the fact that he could be acting for an offeror. I can only say that in a position like that the broker is more likely to say to his client: 'Hold on, there is something coming', and to advise him quietly that there is something better coming and that he ought to hold on on that occasion. J do not hold these enormous fears for crossings. T think there is a lot in the relationship between the individual client and his broker.

Within the game of the financial world there is an enormous amount of personal trust and integrity which undoubtedly prevails in the very marked majority of cases throughout Australia.

Question put:

That Part III of the Bill be agreed to.

The Committee divided.

ADJOURNMENT Gun Clubs and Firearms Control-Proposed
Sale of Belconnen Mall-Grants to the States-Housing-Computer
Equipment-Women Against Rape-Polish Emigration-Plant Breeders' Rights
Motion (by Mr Fife) proposed:
That the House do now adjourn.
Mr MILTON (La Trobe) (10.30)-1 have referred very briefly in the past to the issue of gun clubs. 1 wish to return to this issue tonight. The gun lobby is made up of many different gun clubs in the country'and the firearms traders. I reiterate that 1am in no way opposed to gun clubs or to the legitimate training and practice in the correct use of firearms. But the problem of noise cause by the gun club activities in residential areas is a serious one in my electorate. I want to refer to the Lysterfield Range which affects my constituents in the Belgrave Heights area. Because of extensive change in land use patterns in this area since 1970 when the lease was signed, the gun club now poses severe nuisance value in the area. The number of houses has increased to such an extent that the gun club is now in a residential area. Noise from this range is so bad, and occurs on both weekdays and for many hours at the weekend, that residents frequently have to leave their homes and go out for the day. Residents have taken local action themselves to try to solve this problem, but have been totally unsuccessful. In fact, with the increasing membership of the club, there is now

2352 REPRESENTATIVES 13 May 1981

I
Adjournment i

more noise, from more shoots, and the gun club plans to extend even further.
A local residents action group committed itself to $2,000 worth of sound readings taken by one of Melbourne's most reputable firms. On four occasions in the past few months the readings showed excessive noise at seven different properties throughout the area. Some readings have been taken inside the living rooms of homes and noise levels are such that, under the Australian Standard Specification, there would be immediate community and personal reaction. An expert report to the Shire of Sherbrooke from the Environmental Protection Authority and professional sound consultants last October, stated that: 'from the results of the survey, it is clear that, at present, the Range is imposing an intolerable burden on nearby residents, and we do not believe this should continue.'
There can be no doubt that such noise levels are having a detrimental effect on land values in the area. The local Sherbrooke Shire Council has requested that the gun club take action to lower noise levels, but has found no improvement in noise levels and frequency eminating from the range. It has now resorted to supreme court action on the matter, which involves considerable time, effort and expense from a local council to be involved in. The action has been deemed essential because no relief has been possible through government channels. This is a very real problem to local residents, and is causing severe distress and costing them many thousands of dollars as their home investment is devalued. While I appreciate the need for gun clubs, this particular problem shows that there is a vital need for such organisations to keep in touch with the realities of residential life.
I suggest that an inquiry into the location and nature of gun clubs in the whole of Australia would reveal similar problems in other parts of the country. There is a need to establish a uniform set of standards for the zoning and positioning of these ranges and to remove them from proximity to residential areas. The other aspect of the use of firearms which concerns me is the increased use of such weapons for political and criminal terrorism, as illustrated in the recent assassination of John Lennon in New York and the attempted assassination of President Reagan. Whilst we have not yet reached the stage in this country where our political and other leaders are threatened and killed, and where 200 million guns are in private hands with little or any control over them, as in the United States, the Australian situation is rapidly moving in this direction.

According to Professor Richard Harding, whose book Firearms and Violence in Australian Life has just been published, we are well on the way to reaching the American situation. He describes the domestic gun scene in Australia as 'destructive, volatile, self perpetuating and intractable'. Our political leaders, when asked to comment on events such as the Reagan assassination attempt, claimed smugly that our laws are much tighter here. This may be so. However, the eight Australian police commissioners have pointed out that, through a loophole in regulations, the Israeli Uzi machine pistols and the Russian Kalashnikov assault rifles can be readily imported into this country. This type of weapon is used by terrorists and armed criminals throughout the world. It is favoured by private armies in many countries.
In the context of the increasing dangers to society from all types of firearms, no tightening of regulations would be too severe. The Federal Government must act to ensure that what has happened in the United States of America cannot happen here. Uniform and stringent federal regulations on the use and possession of firearms by individuals and organisations is vitally necessary.
Mr TUCKEY (O'Connor) (10.34)-It will be interesting to consider and comment on the speech which has been made in view of the advice that is often given by honourable members on the other side of the House. In the circumstances which have been mentioned it is a wonder that the constituents were not advised to go and stand in front of the guns. However, I rise on a different matter tonight.
In his statement to the House on the Review of Commonwealth Functions, the Prime Minister (Mr Malcolm Fraser) announced the intention to sell certain commercially-oriented assets. I applaud this announcement. The Opposition denounces it and, in fact, declares it will repurchase any assets sold. I presume it will arbitrarily resume such property purely on the grounds that it was once the property of the Commonwealth. Let us hope this socialistic principle is not to be applied to all other property owners in the Australian Capital Territory. The Opposition further sees this suggestion as being a move to benefit big business in allowing it the opportunity of financial returns on what were government initiatives. This need not be so.
One of the properties concerned is the Belconnen Mall. If honourable members think it is peculiar for a Western Australian to speak on this issue, I think that the silence from the honourable member for Canberra (Mrs Kelly) and the

Adjournment

13 May 1981 REPRESENTATIVES 2353

honourable member for Fraser (Mr Fry) warrants some comments in this House. The Belconnen Mall has a total lettable area of 520,000 square feet. The Mall has 172 separate shops. These vary in size from the Myer Canberra store which is 220,000 square feet to a store of 100 square feet. The four major stores-Myer, G. J. Coles and Co. Ltd, K Mart (Australia) Ltd and Woolworths Ltd-collectively occupy 360,000 square feet while a typical small trader occupies 800 square feet. The tenants of these shops pay rent to the Commonwealth based on their turnover. In other words, all charges to their customers must include this figure. There can be no economy of scale reflected in lower prices.
The tenants have their own association. It met last week to discuss a proposal for the strata titling of the mall. Of the 172 tenants, 118 were represented. They voted unanimously to approach the Government to arrange a strata titles deal and to offer the various shops to the tenants in the first instance. I support this concept entirely. If I need support from the Government, might I quote from the Prime Minister's statement. On page one of his statement, he referred to 'increased opportunities for all'. On page 2, he said:
And we must not forget that it is people, their knowledge and their talents, their enterprise and determination, their belief that they can succeed, who have built Australia.
I support the concept of small government. I equally support the concept of small business. I have ample experience to say that it achieves efficiencies far in excess of big business and creates an enthusiasm to succeed impossible in the impartial corridors of big business. The Government now has a tremendous opportunity to demonstrate its principles of support for private enterprise and support for the individual by sale of the Belconnen Mall to its tenants. Some will say the administrative difficulties make it impossible. I remind them that Columbia has orbitted the earth and returned unscathed.
Some will say the large tenants policy is not to purchase freehold and that certain small tenants will be unable to finance purchase of their section. This could certainly occur. However, it is no deterrent to strata titling. Such areas as are not purchased by their occupiers can be made available to other investors small and large. If we consider the range of areas and consequently variation in purchase prices that will be on offer, it is obvious the type of investors interested in lease back will be considerable. What will the new owneroperator and his customers gain? With fixed capital charges, the tenant will naturally address himself to maximising the turnover of his area.

Increased turnover will improve the capital value of his property and reduce the effect of capital charges on his mark-up. The resulting reduced prices will benefit his customers and presumably increase his turnover further. That is Liberal philosophy at work. At the successful completion of this project, a similar approach should be considered at our airport terminals. The 'ownerdriver' whatever his actual business activity-he might be a contract carpenter, a truck driver or a small shop owner-is the backbone of the Australian economy. I request the Government to take up the challenge at Belconnen and to support this principle. When the sales are completed to those sturdy traders, I challenge the Opposition to try to take them back.
Mr ROBERT BROWN (Hunter) (10.39)-A short time ago, I made reference to the decreasing real value of the total grants that have been made available to the States since this Government first came to office in 1975-76. I indicated on that occasion that I would take the opportunity to pursue the matter further and to explain the developments that have taken place, not only in relation to the total grants being made available to the States, but also, more particularly, to the effect that the States have experienced as a result of the policies of this Government in relation to their general purpose capital grants and their specific purpose grants. On that previous occasion I referred to the fact that during the current year in real terms the total States grants are deficient to the extent of $1,418m compared with the amount the States received in total in 1975-76. I also referred to a statement made by the Minister for Industry and Commerce (Sir Phillip Lynch), at the Liberal Party's New South Wales council meeting in Maitland in March this year. At that conference the Minister also said:
The States can expect little joy from Commonwealth decisions in the short months ahead.
It is becoming increasingly apparent exactly what that statement means. The most recent spectacle was the States' being denied the $70m that both the Prime Minister (Mr Malcolm Fraser) and the Treasurer (Mr Howard) had been authorised by Cabinet to allow to them. Following that, we saw the public humiliation of the States throughout the whole of the Commonwealth. We saw a very serious reaction from the States when they realised the extent to which they had been conned, the reasons they had been conned and the way in which both the Prime Minister and the Treasurer paraded themselves before the media to show how strong and clever they had been, not only at the expense of the State Premiers and

2354 REPRESENTATIVES 13 May 1981

Adjournment

State governments, but also at the expense of the residents in the States.
I wish to develop further the points I made previously in relation to grants. First of all, I will deal specifically with the general purpose capital funds allocated to the States. In 1975-76 general purpose capital funds to the States totalled $1,291m. In the current financial year, 1980-81, general purpose capital funds to the States total $1,307m. That represents an increase during those five years of only 1.2 per cent. In order to maintain their real value in terms of what has happened to costs and prices during that period, they should have increased by 63.4 per cent or $818m. The actual increase that has taken place in those capital funds has not been anything like the $818m necessary in order to retain their real value. They have increased by only $16m during those five years. That means that in this year alone general purpose capital funds to the States are deficient in real terms to the extent of $802m.
The situation in relation to specific purpose payments is essentially the same. In 1975-76 the allocations to the States were $4, 152m. In the current year they are $5,227m. In other words, they have increased by 26 per cent. During that period prices have increased by 63.4 per cent. If specific purpose payments to the States had maintained their real value, they would have increased by $2,632m. In fact, the actual increase was $1 ,075m. Therefore, in this year alone, specific purpose payments to the States are deficient in real terms to the extent of $1 ,567m. The substance and the import of the statements made by the Prime Minister, the Treasurer and the Minister for Trade and Resources (Mr Anthony) are becoming increasingly apparent to the States. There has been a very well orchestrated campaign on the part of the Government to squeeze the States and to put them into a position of being seriously disadvantaged.

In the city of Mildura, which without doubt is one of the finest provincial cities in Australia, this has been indicated by a 28 per cent increase in the issue of housing permits for private dwellings over the last two years. has developed a real confidence in the future viability of our rural and provincial cities. However, Tbelieve it is recognised that housing is a limiting factor in the development of population growth.

Almost every area needs additional skilled tradesmen. Small communitie~ are unable to obtain skilled tradesmen if housing is not avail" able. The recent findings of the Review of Commonwealth Functions have shown us that departments have to move towards cost efficiency. In his statement to the House, the Prime Minister (Mr Malcolm Fraser) announced that every effort will be made to eliminate nonefficient services, to combine common service areas, to make more efficient use of automatic data processing and to decentralise and deregulate management decisions to individual departments.
The Department of Housing and Construction is moving in this direction by acquiring a computer network to ensure efficient delegation of activities. This network requires a central computer with facilities in each region. It will be a link behyeen the central and regional operations. This is a crucial part in the efficient operation of the network. It was one of the main determinants of the Government's decision to acquire the network from IBM Australia Ltd. Criticism of the Government's decision fails to recognise this crucial element. I believe it demonstrates a complete failure by the Opposition to appreciate the importance of ensuring the best equipment for the job.

I wish to speak briefly tonight about the letter which, I think, all honourable members received from the National President of the Returned Services League of Australia in which he made accusations against an organisation called Women Against Rape. The letter stated:

The RSL learned of their plans which were as follows:

1. to bring to Canberra as many members of their group as possible to participate.
2. to march no matter what and carry placards representing the various groups forming part of the women marching, for example, 'Wimmin Against Rape' and 'Women Against Nuclear Energy'.
3. to effectively 'reduce the militarism of the march' by slashing the drums of the bands participating, by damaging their instruments, and by spraying the bandsmen with paint. They planned to make the bands in the march their major target.

4. to obstruct the march by laying down in front of march-

ing veterans so that the veterans would have to walk

over them symbolising 'rape'.

'

5. to deface the Australian War Memorial. It should be

noted that last year the Memorial was defaced by 'per-

sons unknown' but this year the War Memorial in Perth

was desecrated.

These are the hysterical accusations which the RSL and the Minister have made against this women's organisation in Canberra. I just want to read out the response which the organisation has sent to honourable members. It states:

This letter is in response to accusations made against Women Against Rape by the RS.L., the Minister for the Capital Territory, Mr Hodgman, and the Australian Federal Police.

Women Against Rape would like to make it known, yet one more time, that it categorically denies all allegations levelled against it that the group had at any time intended to disrupt the ANZAC Day march last month.

We at no time even discussed the activities suggested (drum slashing, symbolically being walked over by the veterans etc. etc) even in jest, let alone decide to adopt them as tactics on ANZAC Day. From the first five minutes of the first meeting of the organising collective in January, there was a commitment to non-violence.

We went to great lengths to prepare ourselves, to respond non-violently if we were confronted by violence from the police or spectators. All our meetings were open to any

woman who wished to attend; all our decisions were made public through leaflets, letters, media releases and word-ofmouth. Workshops, open to any woman wishing to attend were publicized and run. In these, role playing and discussion were used to help women to prepare themselves to respond non-violently. We are happy to make available to you minutes of all our meetings and all documents relating to our collective.
On ANZAC morning we gathered as planned, marching off silently and in an orderly fashion (after yet again stressing our commitment to non-violence) to join the end of the official march without disruption. The rest of the story is well known. We were stopped and arrests resulted, police using the amended traffic ordinance gazetted for the occasion.
There was no disruption to the official ANZAC parade and it should be noted that the six busloads of interstate women we were supposedly importing for the occasion were nowhere to be seen. Only one-tenth of the sixty one women arrested were from interstate. Neither did any of the women arrested have spray cans of paint, implements with which to slash drums, or anything else, with which to create a disturbance, on their persons.
The RSL, the Minister and the police based their hysterical stand to disallow us marching on what they say was the evidence of an informant. As we know that no such incriminating discussions ever took place we must believe that, either this informant never existed and that the Minister and the RSL are lying, or that if she did exist she lied to them. If the latter is the case, the RSL, the Minister and the police must take responsibility for refusing over three hundred women their rights of free expression on the spurious evidence of one, as yet, unidentified woman.
Of course, it is not mentioned that it may have been a man dressed in drag. The letter continues:
Women Against Rape is at present looking into our legal position in relation to the slanderous and libelous comments made by the Mirtister and the RSL.
Mr COLEMAN (Wentworth) (10.54)-1 want to draw the attention of the House to the grievance of an organisation which is based in my electorate but which speaks for the broken families of Poles and in particular of Polish refugees who have settled in Australia. This is the Committee for Family Reunions, whose address is Post Office Box 104, Woollahra. I am sure that the House is aware that in the last year or two some 2,000 to 3,000 Polish refugees have come to Australia, joining the 120,000 or so Poles who settled here in earlier years. At least 400 of these Poles are husbands who are separated from their wives and children and who have sought unsuccessfully over months and years to get permission for their wives and children who are living in Poland to join them in Australia.
They are obviously being punished by the Polish Government for having left Poland because Poland does not recognise the right of emigration. But worse than that, they are being harassed in Australia and their families are being harassed in Poland. It has been reported by this Committee that some of those who are living in Australia are encouraged or asked to become snoopers on other

2356 REPRESENTATIVES 13 May 1981

Adjournment

Polish refugees and to report their activities to the Polish Consulate. Others are ridiculed on the grounds that their wives do not want to join them, despite all the evidence to the contrary. Mr Boniecki, who is a spokesman for the Australian Committee for Human Rights in the Soviet Union and Eastern Europe, stated:
We have the case of one man whose wife and three-year-old son were refused permission to leave Poland three times. When this man approached the Polish Consul in Sydney at a public function, the Consul laughed off the man's grievance and publicly declared that everyone knew that his wife did not want to join him.
Consequently, to prove the falsity of the statement, the man has obtained a statutory declaration certified by a Polish court that his wife wants to leave the country.
Of course, there has been no change in the attitude of the Po1ish Consul or of the Warsaw Government. These men are being punished for having left Poland, despite the fact that Poland signed the Helsinki Agreement on Human Rights in 1975, not to mention the Universal Declaration on Human Rights of the United Nations. It has refused to issue passports. It has refused this fundamental right.
Some delay in the processing of these matters is understandable. It would not be surprising if it took some weeks or in certain cases some months, but this has taken many months and is going on into years. Late last year the Australian Committee for Human Rights in the Soviet Union and Eastern Europe sent a telegram to the Conference on European Security and Co-operation in Madrid. The telegram stated:
Attention of the Conference is drawn to the specific situation of spouses and children of hundreds of Polish refugees recently settled in Australia. These families still in Poland are repeatedly refused passports so that they can leave Poland and be reunited with their families in Australia. Please press the Polish authorities to respect these basic human rights in the spirit of the Helsinki Agreements and to allow these families to leave immediately.
There has been no response from Poland. There has certainly been a response in Australia. There have been hunger strikes. There is a fear that there will be more serious responses by these people in Australia who have been unable to bring their wives and children-in some cases very young children-from Poland. We know that the Polish Government is divided. We know that there are hard liners who want the Soviet Union to occupy the country in the manner of Czechoslovakia in the 1960s. We also know that there are others who take a more progressive line. There are some in Australia who would say that we should not be embarrassing the Polish Government in the present situation, but it seems to me that it is so simple a matter for the progressive element in the Polish Government to agree to give

passports to those families that we should be pressing them. The Polish Government could do one simple thing to earn the sympathy of Australians and that is allow these families to be reunited. The Polish Government is seeking credit from the Australian Government as it is seeking credit from other governments throughout the world. The least that the Australian Government can do is support these settlers, who will in due course become citizens of this country, and ask the Polish Government to cease punishing them and allow the families to be reunited in Australia.
Mr HOWE (Batman) (10.59)-1 want to draw attention briefly to a petition which 1 presented yesterday on behalf of a co-operative group in the north-west of Tasmania known as TARGA. That group collected 2,500 signatures concerning the issue of plant breeders' rights. The group holds very strong views about the subject. It is concerned about the proposed legislation, which will restrict free growing and the exchange and sale of non-chemically treated seeds, and believes that a decline in the genetic diversity of plants was evidenced in Europe following the implementation of such legislation. The group believes that it is important that this issue be canvassed in full scale public debate. I believe that the group would argue that there are several reasons why the plant breeders' rights legislation ought to be opposed. It would argue, firstly, that seeds are a public resource; secondly, that seed patenting effectively allows large corporations to control seed production and sales; and, thirdly, that germ plasm, the basic seed used in breeding programs, is hoarded by these corporations and exchanges of seed and scientific information slow down. 1 believe that this point of view that this group is seeking to disseminate is important. 1 think it is unfortunate that sometimes all the work that goes into collecting signatures for petitions is given such brief attention in the House. 1just wanted to place on record in the time available the fact that this group had done the work and that there were so many signatures on the petition and to draw attention to the substance of the petition.

the need for computer technology as a tool for efficient government administration. At the outset I want to say that I wholeheartedly endorse his sentiments. network.

H. Brookes. These studies confirmed the original finding that the substantially higher technical risks associated with alternative solutions could not be accepted, The Government is satisfied that the expert reviews carried out have ensured the best possible solution to meet the needs of the Department of Housing and Construction.

Over the past few months views have been expressed in reports by the media concerning a tender for the data processing requirements for the Department of Housing and Construction, Canberra, culminating in comments and statements made in a report on page 1 of the Pacific Computer Weekly of 23-29 January 1981. Univac's association with governments, both Federal and State, is conducted on a professional basis, with the supply of equipment and services to them being of the highest possible standard. At all times Sperry Univac has received the respected assistance, courtesy and advice from all government departments. We have always found the actions of various government members to be of the highest professional standard.
As honourable members will be aware, there have been many attempts to criticise my Department's computer purchase. Attempts were made on 8 September 1980 at the House of Representatives Committee on Expenditure; in this chamber on three occasions during September 1980; and in the Pacific Computer Weekly of 23-29 January 1981. I regret to say that in this chamber today yet another attempt was made to criticise that purchase and to criticise and question the integrity of the Public Service and the tendering processes which were followed in the computer procurement. I hope that this restatement of the facts will put these unfounded allegations to rest once and for all. I resent the aspersions cast on the integrity of officers of my Department who are men of outstanding ability, honesty and sincerity of purpose. These aspersions were cast earlier today by the honourable member for Reid (Mr Uren).

REPLY TO A REQUEST FOR DETAILED INFORMATION
Department of the Parliamentary Library On 28 April 1981, Mr Mountford asked Mr Speaker-

(2) How many serial titles were acquired by the Library in each of these years.

(3) How does the Library follow up the non-receipt of single issues of acquired serials, in order to maintain a complete collection, when was the practice adopted and what was the practice prior to that.
Mr Speaker-The answer to the honourable member's question is as follows:

(2) 1969-70: 4500 (approximately); 1979-80: 9000 (approximately) .
(3) Follow-up is by means of a 'claim' to the supplier to provide the missing issue(s). There has never been any practice other than this.

The following answers to questions were circulated:

Social Security Appeals
(Question No. 21)
Mr Holding asked the Minister representing the Attorney-General, upon notice, on 26 November 1980:
(1) Further to the Attorney-General's answer to Question No. 5318 (Hansard, 22 May 1980, page 3197), and the report on Social Security Appeals made to the Attorney-General by the Administrative Review Council in June 1980, what was the situation as at 25 November 1980;
(2) Will the Administrative Review Council's report on Social Security Appeals be published, as recommended by the Council in paragraph 102 of its Fourth Annual Report.
Mr Viner-The Attorney-General has provided the following answer to the honourable member's question:
(l) The Administrative Review Council reported to me on Social Security Appeals in June 1980. The Council's report is under consideration by the Government. In the meantime, the Administrative Appeals Tribunal (Social Services Act) Regulations have been amended to extend the rights of appeal to the Administrative Appeals Tribunal in social security matters.
(2) A summary of the Administrative Review Council's report on Social Security Appeals was included in the Council's Fourth Annual Report, which I have tabled. I expect to table the report on Social Security Appeals in the very near future.
Assistance to Inventors Scheme
(Question No. 47)
Mr Les Johnson asked the Minister for Science and Technology, upon notice, on 26 November 1980;
(I) What commercial results have been reached by individuals assisted under the Assistance to Inventors Scheme since 1975.
(2) What measures does the Government take to assist inventors to obtain commercial results for inventions initially attracting grants under the Scheme.
(3) Is the Government satisfied with the current operation of the Scheme and the commercial results that have flowed therefrom; if not, are measures proposed to improve the scheme; if so, what are they.
Mr Thomson-The answer to the honourable member's question is as follows:
(1) Financial assistance was first providcd under the Assistance to Inventors Scheme in 1975-76. To 31 December 1980 a total of 50 inventors grants had been made under the scheme amounting in aggregate to $225,776. A review of the progress of the individual grant-assisted inventions revealed:
(a) Eight of the inventions are currently in production with aggregate sales in 1980-81 estimated to amount to $500,000;
(b) Ten of the inventions have been advanced to the stage where they are considered ready for commercial production and the involvement of appropriate manufacturers is actively being sought;

(c) Twenty-seven of the inventions are still under development with a view to their eventual commercial production; and
(d) Pive of the inventions have been found not to be viable and their further development abandoned.
(2) The Assistance to Inventors Scheme provides for grant assistance to enable the development of worthy patentable inventions to the stage where the technical feasibility can be established and the potential usefulness demonstrated in practice. This primarily involves assistance to finance the construction and testing of a demonstration prototype.
The hasic rationale underlying the Scheme is that at this stage the invcntor should be in a position to both lodge a complete patent speciGcation and to pursue commercialisation of the invention by, say, entering into an arrangement with an appropriate manufacturer. No direct assistance is provided under the Scheme in respect of the subsequent stages involved in obtaining commercial results from grant-assisted inventions.
The Government does however recognise the need for sound and responsible policies to promote the commercialisation of Australian invention. It has implemented a number of pilot innovation programs which, together with the Assistance to Inventors Scheme, will provide a base of practical experience on which to evaluate longer term policy options for the realisation of the full industrial and employment benefits of Australian inventiveness.
Assistance is made available under the pilot innovation programs for the following measures directed at facilitating the realisation of commerical results from developed inventions:
(a) Establishment, in conjunction with State Chambers of Manufactures, of 'Innovation Centres' to promote greater interaction between private inventors and manufacturers;
(b) Organisation, in conjunction with interested Australian companies and academic institutions, of 'Adventure Workshops in Innovation and Entrepreneurship' designed to provide the opportunity for selected Australian graduates to develop the entrepreneurial skills required to progress an invention to the market; and
(c) Investigation, under the 'Pilot Enterprise Development Program', of practical means for providing access by private inventors to the full spectrum of expertise required to develop and implement strategies for the creation of new technology-based firms.
(3) The commercial results arising from grant-assisted inventions, as indicated in (1) above, are considered to demonstrate the cost-effectiveness of funds which have been made available for the Assistance to Inventors Scheme. To this extent the Government is satisfied with the results that have flowed from the Scheme. The Government is however concerned to ensure the improved administrative effectiveness of the Scheme. In this respect, the following measures were set out in the schedule of decisions arising from the Review of Commonwealth Functions tabled by the Prime Minister on 30 April 1981:
(a) Administration of the Scheme to be streamlined;
(b) Abolition of the Australian Inventions Advisory Committee; and

2360 REPRESENTATIVES 13 May 1981

Answers to Questions

(c) Review of the Scheme by persons from the private sector.
Welfare Expenditure in Australian Capital Territory
(Question No. 56)
Dr Everingham asked the Minister for the Capital Territory, upon notice, on 26 November 1980:
(I) what grants, loans or concessions are made by his Department or others for (a) health, (b) education, (c) welfare, (d) development, (e) disaster relief or (f) other purposes in the Australian Capital Territory on the basis of matching allocations from other Departments such as are matched in the States by State allocations.
(2) what is the (a) maximum and (b) minimum rate per capita of such matching allocations by Australian States and Territories for (i) women's refuges, (ii) youth refuges, (iii) remedial and special education, (iv) self-help activities for the unemployed incorporating economic, educational, cultural and communal activities and (v) other voluntary community organisations.
Mr Hodgman-The answer to the honourable member's question is as follows:
I am informed by my Department that-
The Department of the Capital Territory has consulted with officers of the Capital Territory Health Commission and the ACT Schools Authority and advises in respect to question (l) that there are no grants, loans or concessions made on the basis of matching allocations from other Departments by the Department of the Capital Territory, the Commission or the Authority for the purposes listed.
Consequently there can be no answer to question (2).
There are however arrangements in the ACT whereby community organisations seek and obtain assistance along comparable lines to assistance provided to such organisations by governments in the States.
It is generally the practice for community organisations to apply for assistance direct to the Commonwealth Government Department or Authority responsible for that particular matter. There are nine such Departments and Authorities which make grants to ACT community organisations for a wide range of activities.
The grants made by these Departments and Authorities over the last five financial years were provided in answer to question No. 4541 in 1979. Since then and following consultations with other Ministers, the Department of the Capital Territory has established an Interdepartmental Committee to act as a clearing house. This Committee provides the opportunity for the exchange of information and expertise on applications for grants which are being considered by these Departments and Authorities. These arrangements should facilitate co-ordination and rationalisation of grants to ACT organisations.
Australian Capital Territory House of Assembly
(Question No. 171)
Mr Scholes asked the Minister for the Capital Territory a question, upon notice, on 25 November 1980:
Do: (a) members of the Australian Capital Territory House of Assembly have protection against litigation in respect of matters raised as part of the proceedings of the Assembly, and

(b) witnesses giving evidence before committees of the Assembly have any legal protection;
if not will he take action to provide reasonable protection?
Mr Hodgman-The answers to the honourable member's questions are as follows:
I am informed by my Department that the honourable member's question calls for an expression of legal opinion and, as the honourable member will be aware, the Standing Orders of the House of Representatives provide that questions shall not ask for a legal opinion. I am, however, able to inform the Honourable Member that the whole question of the Australian law on defamation is under review by the Standing Committee of Attorneys-General and that any reform in the law relating to privilege for the ACT House of Assembly will need to await the outcome of this review. I should perhaps add that I am concerned about the position of the Assembly following the recent Supreme Court case involving a member of the Assembly and I am anxious to resolve the situation as quickly as possible.

Rosemount Repatriation Hospital
(Question No. 256)
Dr Everingham asked the Minister representing the Minister for Veterans' Affairs, upon notice, on 3 December 1980:
(1) Has the closure of the Rosemount Repatriation Hospital, Queensland, been discussed with organisations representing (a) employees, (b) patients and/ or (c) relatives.
(2) Will an equal number of beds be available after the anticipated closure as readily and promptly at all times for simiJar purposes as beds are available at Rosemount.
(3) Will staff ceilings be affected by the closure; if so, will individual attention to patients be reduced, including (a) catering for personal dietary preferences, (b) supportive counselling and (c) other person-to-personrapport between staff members and the patients and families they serve.
Mr Thomson-The Minister for Veterans' Affairs has provided the following answer to the honourable member's question:
(1 ) Yes. Prior to the decision to close the Rosemoun t Repatriation Hospital being taken and announced, a number of discussions took place between senior officers of my department, ex-service and employee organisations and others. Apart from some concern about a few points, there was general acceptance that transfer of the functions to the Repatriation General Hospital, Greenslopes, would be for the benefit of patients.
Since the closure was announced, discussions have taken place with a wider range of organisations, particularly the ex-service organisations and the Queensland Branch of the Hospital Employees' Federation, as well as patients and their relatives. The proposed closure has been widely publicised and all interested parties have had the opportunity to present their views to my Department or direct to myself.
Many different issues have been raised in the discussions about the closure of Rosemount Repatriation Hospital and have ranged across such topics as the placement of individual patients and the future use of the property and its facilities. These discussions are continuing and many of the issues will not be decided until the transfer of the functions of Rosemount Repatriation Hospital to the Repatriation General Hospital, Greenslopes, is about to, or has taken place. On present indications this will be in late May 1981.
(2) Currently, the Rosemount Repatriation Hospital has some 60 beds available for use. When this hospital is closed, two wards, each of 32 beds, will be opened at Repatriation General Hospital, Greenslopes. These beds will be available as promptly and in the same way as beds have been available at Rosemount.
(3) The functions and staff ceiling resources of Rosemount Repatriation Hospital will be transferred to the Repatriation General Hospital, Greenslopes. The treatment provided to patients will be improved as a direct result of the clinical supervision and services available at an acute-care general hospital and there will be no diminution of attention to individual patients and their families.
Historic Aircraft
(Question No. 361)
Mr Les Johnson asked the Minister for Home Affairs and Environment, upon notice, on 4 December 1980:
(1) What measures are taken to determine the historic worth of aircraft.

(2) What are the names and qualifications of any experts who advise the Government on such aircraft.
Mr Wilson-The answer to the honourable member's question is as follows:
I am informed by my Department:
(I) The following criteria have been established to assist in assessing the national historic significance of aircraft:
A close association of the aircraft with a famous Australian or organisation.
Export of the aircraft would mean the loss of a unique or rare type of aircraft to Australia.
Aircraft manufactured in Australia and an important example of industrial technology of a particular period.
Aircraft which have been used by the Australian Forces.
Aircraft presently in commercial use in Australia or still in use by the Defence Forces.
Aircraft which were operated from Australia by Australia's allies in World War II.
Aircraft operated by enemy forces against Australian forces.
Aircraft which have not been modified to such extent that their integrity as an example of type has been permanently destroyed.
Aircraft of a type not represented in other public collections in Australia, in particular the Service collections or the Australian War Memorial.
(2) Expert advice is sought from-Department of Defence, The Air Force Association, Australian War Memorial, Antique Aeroplane Association of Australia, The Australian Society for Aero-historical Preservation.
Mining Equipment
(Question No. 406)
Mr Jacobi asked the Minister for Industry and Commerce, upon notice, on 24 February 1981:
(I) Has his attention been drawn to the Joint Coal Board report for 1979-80 which notes that for expansion of the coal industry to take place to its full potential by the year 2000 it will be necessary that, among other things, local industry play a greater part in the servicing and manufacture of mining equipment.
(2) If so, what steps will the Government take to assist industry and ensure that this recommendation is achieved.
Sir Phillip Lynch The answer to the honourable member's question is as follows:
(I) Yes.
(2) The Government is pursuing policies for manufacturing industry which will encourage and enabJe firms to take advantage of opportunities presented by the upsurge in Australia's resource development.
It is not for the Government to take steps to guarantee that the local mining equipment industry plays a greater part in the expansion of the coal industry, or for that matter any other mining industry, but rather to ensure the existence of an environment in which the manufacturers of mining equipment may themselves seize opportunities of the kind alluded to in the Board's report.

2362 REPRESENTATIVES 13 May 1981

Answers to Questions

Central to this approach are the Government's policies to encourage the development of competitive manufacturing industries involving specialisation in particular skills and products and use of advanced technology.
In the case of the mining equipment industry, assistance is available via the tariff. In addition a number of generally available measures such as the investment allowance and the Industrial Research and Development Incentives Scheme can be of help to manufacturers seeking to improve their production processes and keep pace with technological advances to maintain international competitiveness.

Alternative Fuel Technologies
(Question No. 410)
Mr Jacobi asked the Minister representing the Minister for National Development and Energy, upon notice, on 24 February 1981:
Does the Government accept the conclusion of the National Energy Advisory Committee in its 9th report that Government initiatives on alternative on alternative fuel technologies will be required given (a) the large capital investments required for such alternative fuel processes, (b) the reluctance of investors to provide capital for processes in which there has been little or no commercial experience and (c) the inevitable influence of Government policies on liquid fuel prices and on the costs of production and processing; if so, what initiatives will the Government take and when; if not, why not.
Mr Anthony-The Minister for National Development and Energy has provided the following answer to the honourable member's question:
The issues identified in NEAC Ryport No.9 Liquid Fuels: Longer Term Needs, Prospects and Issues were further developed, as they relate to alternative liquid fuels, in NEAC Report No. 12 Alternative Liquid Fuels. This report, which was released on 2 February 1981, recommends that the Government should vigorously encourage the development of alternative liquid fuels, but that commercial criteria should be applied to any decision to produce these fuels on a significant scale. The Government's fundamental responsibility in the development of alternative liquid fuels is seen as being to ensure an investment climate conducive to the commercial development of such fuels by the private sector.
The Government has already introduced policies which are consistent with this approach. These policies include the following elements:
Pricing-The Government has a policy of pricing indigenous conventional crude oil to refiners at the import parity price, which is ased on the official price of Saudi Arabian Light Crude oil. As a consequence of this policy, the products of synthetic fuel plants will be competing against conventional petroleum products whose prices are based on the import parity price for crude oil.
Tax-Synfuels industries would be eligible for existing tax concessions, which include the following (rates shown are those proposed in the Prime Minister's Parliamentary Statementof30Apri11981):
the generalIS per cent investment allowance;
the 18 per cent loading on depreciation allowances on eligible plant ordered after 19 August 1980; and
concessions to mining industries for exploration and development insofar as applicable to exploration and development of coal, oil shale, natural gas or other minerals used as feedstocks for synfuel processes.
In addition, the Government has removed excise from ethanol and LPG used for transport fuel.
Foreign Investment-The Commonwealth has a well established and clearly stated foreign investment policy which welcomes foreign investment in Australia because of the contribution it makes to the development of Australia's industries and resources. The policy seeks to encourage foreign investment on a basis that recognises the needs and aspirations of Australians. The Government considers that Australians should have opportunities, wherever practicable, to participate with foreign investors in major projects.

Answers to Questions

13 May 1981 REPRESENTATIVES 2363

Loan Council/Infrastructure Finance-In June 1978, the Australian Loan Council introduced a special category of loan programs for semi-government authorities to facilitate the planning and implementation of major projects of importance for development, with financing requirements which could not be accommodated within normal programs.
Environment-The Commonwealth has well established and clear requirements for assessing environmental impacts of proposals which involve the application of Commonwealth powers. This permits early identification and resolution of the environmental problems of any major development, including alternative liquid fuels projects.
Research and Development-The Government funds a wide variety of investigations into various alternative fuels under NERDDC. So far, over $15 million have been committed to synfuels research under this program.
Vietnam Veterans
(Question No. 498)
Mr Holding asked the Minister representing the Minister for Veterans' Affairs, upon notice, on 24 February 1981:
(1) Has the Minister's attention been drawn to the article headed '470 Viet Veterans Suicide' on page 1 of the Melbourne Truth of 17 January 1981.
(2) If so, is the Minister able to confirm the figure of 470 Vietnam veterans suicides and is it a fact, as alleged in the article, that this figure represents approximately a hundredfold increase in the number of suicides in the general population.
(3) What occurrences between 13 October 1980, when the Prime Minister in a letter to Mrs P. Voltz of Oakleigh, Vic., stated that the Government recognised that existing health and welfare programs adequately covered the needs of veterans, and 14 January 1981, when the Minister made a statement on free hospital treatment offered to Vietnam veterans, led to a change in Government policy.
(4) What arrangements for the new counselling service for Vietnam veterans as announced by the Minister on 14 January 1981 exist at 24 February 1981, including details of (a) numbers and names of persons providing counselling, (b) their locations, (c) whether they are attached to the Department of Veterans' Affairs; if so, on what basis, (d) their qualifications, professional and otherwise and (e) the numbers of members of the Vietnam Veterans Action Association who are directly involved in the counselling services.

(8) If so, what are the reasons for the apparent discrepancies between these 2 statements.

(1) Yes.

(2) No. To confirm or deny the number of suicides, it would be necessary to identify all Vietnam veterans who have died and investigate the cause of death. This information is not currently available, but mortality studies, including study of deaths by suicide, will be undertaken by the Commonwealth Institute of Health in the course of the epidemiological study of Vietnam veterans.
(3) The Government has kept under examination the needs of Vietnam veterans and the means available to meet them. Concern was felt at statements made in late 1980 about ill health of a number of Vietnam veterans and claims that their emotional states of mind put their lives at risk. In an effort to help veterans with any immediate health and emotional problems, the Government took action to provide the urgent hospital treatment and the counselling assistance outlined in my Statement of 14 January 1981.
(4) An interim counselling service was immediately set up on 14 January 1981 pending the consideration about a more permanent counselling structure.

Small Business Bankruptcies
(Question No. 537)
Mr Free asked the Minister for Business and Consumer Affairs upon notice, on 24 February 1981:
How many small businesses went bankrupt or were liquidated in each of the financial years from 1972-1973 to 1979-1980 inclusive.
Mr Moore-The answer to the honourable member's question is as follows:
Neither the bankruptcy legislation nor the companies legislation contains any definition of a small business. The existing statistical information available in relation to individual and corporate insolvencies has not been collected in a way which would enable the presentation of statistics on the basis of any meaningful distinction based on the size of the business.

The annual report on the operation of the Bankruptcy Act now contains statistics on the number of business bankruptcies, which are defined as 'bankruptcies of employers and persons engaged in a trade, business or profession on their own account' (the remaining bankruptcies are treated as nonbusiness bankruptcies).
The statistics of business bankruptcies as defined in these annual reports for the relevant period are as follows:
1972-73-n.a. 1973-74-n.a. 1974-75-1,252 1975-76-1,243 1976-77-1,270 1977-78-1,752 1978-79-1,986 1979-80-2,530
In considering these statistics the following points should be borne in mine:

2366 REPRESENTATIVES 13 May 1981

Answers to Questions

Statistics for 1972-73 and 1973-74 are not available in a form which would enable reliable infomation to be given.
The term 'bankruptcy' is used to refer to a matter that has been the subject of a separate debtor's petition or creditor's petition. It is possible to present a single petition covering two or more partners or joint debtors. For example, in the ACT, New South Wales and Victoria for the period of I January to 30 June 1979 there were 941 bankruptcies and 1,136 bankrupts.
The figures in this table refer to petitions under Part IV of the Bankruptcy Act. They do not cover Part X or Part XI matters.
Most liquidations of bodies corporate are carried out under the winding up provisions of the companies legislation of each State and Territory. Liquidations commenced under the ACT Companies Ordinance during the financial years 1972-73 to 1979-80 were as follows:
