In interpreting these figures the following points may be of assistance to the honourable member:
As is obvious the majority of liquidations arc categorised as 'members' voluntary'. All liquidations so categorised relate to companies that are solvent. Some are indicative of group rationalisations rather than company failures.
The other two categories of liquidations normally relate to companies that are unable to pay their debts as they become due.
These figures do not indicate the liquidations of 'foreign companies' operating in the ACT.
Particulars of these liquidations would be held in the home jurisdiction of the body corporate concerned and would be the responsibility of the appropriate State Minister.
The figures do not distinguish between ACT incorporate companies operating within the ACT and those operating elsewhere.
It is estimated that a substantial number of ACT incorporated companies do not operate in the Territory.
The figures do not distinguish between companies that conduct a small business, companies that conduct a large business, or companies that do not conduct any business at all. For example, some companies are investment companies.
Many company failures are not represented by liquidations but by companies struck off the register of companies by the Registrar pursuant to section 308 of the Companies Ordinance.
These companies have ceased to operate or to carry on business. Normally they have lost all of their assets and frequently have substantial liabilities. There are generally no parties interested in instigating liquidation proceedings as there are no assets which could be realised for the purpose of paying a dividend.

STD and ISD Telephone Services
(Question No. 555)
Mr Beazley asked the Minister for Communications, upon notice, on 24 February 1981:
(1) Have 4,000 Western Australians opted out of the STD system in their telephone service?
(2) How many Western Australian subscribers have to this point requested to opt out of the international subscribers dialling service?
(3) In view of the fact that many of those who opt out of both services do so to avoid the consequences of being confronted with large bills resulting from their own excess use or the use made by others of their phones, will he waive the $3 disconnection charge and treat future requests as part of Telecom's normal service.
Mr Sinclair-The answer to the honourable member's question is as follows:
(1) There are some 400,000 subscribers in Western Australia connected to automatic exchanges from which STD is available. While precise figures are not kept, around 12,000 have elected to have their services barred from STD. There is a charge of $3 per annum involved.
(2) Telecom Australia provides the international subscribers dialling service only to those subscribers who apply to be connected to such a service.
(3) Advances in technology are enabling Telecom to introduce a system known as Call Charge Record (CCR) which will automatically record details of subscriber dialled international calls. That is, Telecom will be in a position to provide customers with the same degree of accountability on ISD calls that is currently provided with operator connected trunk or international calls. In other words, Te1ecom is automating an already existing facility.
Should any subscriber wish to take less than full advantage of the telephone service, there is a standard fee of $3 per annum which will ensure that access from the service is restricted in a prescribed way. The provision of access barring facilities involves clerical costs in processing the application, the equipment installation itself and continuing maintenance checks to ensure the correct operation of the facility. Telecom advises that the fee of $3 per annum does not cover these costs.

Social Welfare Supplementary Assistance
(Question No. 623)
Mrs Darling asked the Minister representing the Minister for Social Security, upon notice, on 25 February 1981:
(1) Further to the Minister's reply to question No. 142 (Hansard, 4 December 1980, page 470), by how much has the cost of average rental accommodation increased since the last increase to supplementary assistance was made on 14 November 1974.
(2) Is it a fact that the cost of Queensland Housing Commission rental accommodation has increased from $16.25 per week in June 1974 to approximately $45 per week in June 1980.
(3) Will the Government give serious consideration to increasing the supplementary assistance in line with inflation in rents as indicated by increases in State Housing Commission rents; if not, why not.

Mr Hunt-The Minister for Social Security has provided the following answer to the honourable member's question:
(1) The private housing rent component of the Consumer Price Index increased by 71 per cent between the December quarters of 1974 and 1980 and the government housing component by 198 per cent. However, on average, government rents are still much lower than private rents, as shown by the recently published Australian Bureau of Statistics Survey of Housing Occupancy and Costs, Australia, August 1980, which estimated that, at the survey date, the average weekly rent of houses in State capital cities was $32.43 in the case of government landlords and $56.17 in the case of private landlords (other than the individual's employer).
(2) These rent levels are determined by the Queensland Housing Commission. Recent policy is that they be marketrelated. It is my understanding that the rents quoted by the honourable member are broadly correct and reflect rents charged by the Commission for three-bedroom houses.
(3) The level of supplementary assistance is determined in relation to other priorities in the Budget context. Although changes in rent levels are a factor to be considered in assessing the levels of this supplement for rent paying pensioners with little or no private income, it should be noted that pensioners and beneficiaries who are tenants" of State-Housing Commissions may avail themselves of the assistance provided by those authorities by way of rental rebates or rent reductions.

As at 25 February 1981 Dr A. Kameron, B.Sc., M.sc., Ph.D., from W. D. Scott and Co., was engaged on a consultancy basis to provide project planning and control assistance with the investigation.
(3) Instruments and questionnaires are currently in final draft awaiting printing. Addresses of persons selected by statisticians for a pilot study are available. Mailing facilities and interviewers are being organised in anticipation of the beginning of a pilot study, which is the next step in the plan. Assessment of the pilot study results will determine the method of the main study.
(4) The study team has acquired lists of Army personnel who served in Vietnam. Complete information regarding Navy, RAAF and public servants has not yet been received. Numbers are sufficient for the aims of the investigation provided the response by veterans and their families to the survey questionnaire is at the required statistical level.
(5) See (4) above. No veterans or public servants have been examined or assessed to date. No examination or assessments can proceed until the pilot study commences. No valid assessments can be made until the major study of approximately 40 000-42000 Vietnam veterans has been completed.

Funding for Development of Medical Aids and Equipment
(Question No. 773)
Mrs Kelly asked the Minister for the Capital Territory, upon notice, on 11 March 1981:
(1) Has he taken action to ensure that inventors and manufacturers in the Australian Capital Territory are being supported and encouraged by being given access to funding for the development of medical aids and equipment, such as wheelchairs, for disabled persons; if so, what action.
(2) What technical or commercially viable projects for improved equipment for disabled persons in the ACT have received funding in this International Year of Disabled Persons.
(3) What funds are available for this purpose for the remainder of 1981.
Mr Hodgman-The answer to the honourable member's question is as follows:
(1) I am advised by my Department that action has been taken by the ACT Advisory Committee for IYDP to encourage the involvement of all parts of our community in assisting disabled persons and is already well advanced. This has included, with the co-operation of the Confederation of ACT Industry, fostering of awareness and support of IYDP by way of an employer education programme directed to some 1,400 outlets. On an even broader plane through the Advisory Committee's Newsletter 'Delta', organisations and individuals are being encouraged to develop projects designed to assist disabled persons and to seek advice and assistance where that is needed.
The Assistance to Inventors Scheme administered by my colleague the Minister for Science and Technology, serves as an excellent means of encouraging individual initiative and provides for financial assistance, by way of a grant, of up to $10,000 for development of anyone project including the support and testing of prototypes of inventions. Aids to assist disabled persons are covered by this scheme.
(2) A number of requests have been received by the Advisory Committee Secretariat in recent months for funding to assist the development of innovative projects aimed at assisting disabled persons in the ACT community-surveillance devices to safeguard autistic children, alarms to assist blind and

Answers to Questions

13 May 1981 REPRESENTATIVES 2369

visually impaired people crossing traffic intersections and a wheelchair with special features, for the physically disabled. A grant of $700 has already been paid to the autistic Children's Association of the ACT under a Special Childrens' Services Programme allocation to mark IYDP, to assist the development of various items of prototype equipment. A number of other inventions are being examined by the Advisory Committee for possible funding.
(3) As I have indicated, a well established scheme designed to encourage and support inventions operates at a national level. ACT inventors have access to that scheme. No funds have been set aside in the ACT specifically to assist the development of technical aids and equipment for disabled persons.

The increase in prices paid for northside land reflects the market demand for an increasingly scarce commodity as the
number of sites that could be offered for lease decreased. On the other hand the fluctuation in prices for southside land has occurred when less attractive sites with lower reserve prices have been offered for sale.

(2) On present indications it is expected that about 2,000 standard residential leases will be offered for sale during 1980-81. Apart from about sixty blocks on the northside of Canberra, this land is all located on the southside of Canberra.

(3) Reserve prices are fixed to recover overall the cost of providing serviced sites. Since no major contracts for residentialland servicing have been let since 1976, it is difficult to predict trends in costs, but because of inflation it must be expected that the reserve prices placed on sites will move upwards for both northside and southside land.

Noise Pollution in the Australian Capital Territory
(Question No. 777)
Mrs Kelly asked the Minister for the Capital Territory, upon notice, on 11 March 1981:

What action has he taken to (a) prepare and (b) implement legislation for the control of noise pollution in the Australian Capital Territory.
Mr Hodgman-The answer to the honourable member's question is as follows:
I am informed by my Department that instructions for the preparation of an Ordinance to control noise pollution in the Australian Capital Territory were forwarded to the Attorney General's Department in April 1979. These instructions sought the preparation of legislation to reflect policy advice received from the Inter-Departmental Committee on Environmental Quality in the Australian Capital Territory. The Attorney-General's Department has raised a number of problems with the proposals set out in those instructions, the consideration of which has necessitated a review of complex technical matters which must be dealt with in the proposed Ordinance. Further instructions will be sent when these problems have been resolved.
I am aware of the feeling within the community that the introduction of effective noise control legislation is required as a matter of urgency and have, accordingly, requested that the problems be resolved and the legislation prepared as a matter of high priority.
Petrol Price in Canberra
(Question No. 778)
Mrs Kelly asked the Minister for the Capital Territory, upon notice, on 11 March 1981.
What was the recommended and/or average price of super grade petrol in Canberra as at 30 June for each year from 1975 to 1980.
Mr Hodgman-The answer to the honourable member's question is as follows:
I am informed by my Department that the price of petroleum products was subject to price control in the Australian Capital Territory as at both 30 June 1975 and 30 June 1976. The prescribed maximum retail prices for premium grade petrol at those dates were as follows:
June 1975: Ampol-68.2 cents per gallon; Golden Fleece-67.9 cents per gallon; In any other case-67.2 cents per gallon.
June 1976: EITHER Ampol or Golden Fleece-17.5 cents per litre; In any other case-17,4 cents per litre; OR Golden Fleece-79.6 cents per gallon; Ampol-79.5 cents per gallon; Mobil-79.1 cents per gallon; In any other case-78.9 cents per gallon.
Price controls on petrol were discontinued on 26 October 1976. Since that date the price charged for petrol has varied between retail outlets and, therefore, I am unable to provide a firm answer to the actual or average petrol prices on the 30 June of each subsequent year. However, from information obtained for Consumer Price Index purposes, the Australian Bureau of Statistics compiles details of average prices in the capital cities for the week day closest to the 15th of the first month of each quarter.
The average retail prices for petrol in Canberra as estimated by ABS for the respective June quarters are as follows:
June 1977-17.8 cents per litre June 1978-19.0 cents per litre June 1979-25.3 cents per litre June 1980-34.9 cents per litre.

Alumina Refinery at Wagerup, Western Australia
(Question No. 913)
Mr Howe asked the Minister for Trade and Resources, upon notice, on 26 March 1981:
(1) Has the Minister's attention been drawn to Alcoa's decision to delay work on its alumina refinery at Wagerup, WA.
(2) Is the Minister able to say whether the principal reason for this decision was a downturn in demand for aluminium in the United States of America.
(3) If this was the reason, what other projects in the aluminium industry may be affected by this downturn in demand.
(4) What assessment has been made by the Government of the likely demand for aluminium in the USA and Japan through to the mid 1980's.
(5) Is there any evidence of the fall in international demand affecting the price of alumina and aluminium on international markets.
(6) What published studies does the Minister rely on to form his assessments of trends in the international aluminium markets in the 1980's.
Mr Anthony-The answer to the honourable member's question is as fonows:
(1) Yes.
(2) and (3) The generally accepted view among aluminium indusry experts is that the United States aluminium market has a considerable influence on the fortunes of the aluminium

industry throughout the world, including the international alumina market. However, it would be misleading to attribute the decision by Alcoa of Australia directly to a short-term downturn in United States demand for aluminium. According to a spokesman from Alcoa of Australia the decision was due to a softening of the international alumina market.
Commodity markets are normally subject to cyclical influences on demand. In 1979 there was a marked world shortage of aluminium which resulted in a rapid run down of producers' stocks. Throughout 1980 stocks were replenished to normal levels. These influences resulted in aluminium producers running smelters at high capacity and alumina producers streamlining production facilities to produce additional tonnage. The current alumina over-supply, which is likely to persist into 1982 is partly a result of the above chain of events and partly a result of failure of the metal market to sustain growth projections in the short term.
Alcoa's decision is the result of the company's commercial judgement. According to the company's thinking, there would be little point in hastening such a project to completion before the market has returned to optimum conditions.
1 am not aware of other projects which may be delayed because of market factors. Taking both demand and supply factors into account, it is confidently predicted that the outlook for Australia's alumina and aluminium remains quite favourable and that any delays in completion of projects will be temporary. It is interesting to reflect that the construction of the Alcoa Pinjarra refinery was also undertaken in a situation of market downturn, but subsequent recovery in demand fully accommodated the tonnage produced.
(4) Current indications are that both the US. and Japanese aluminium demand are expected to grow at about 5 per cent per annum over the coming decade although yearly variations are almost certain.
(5) I am informed that alumina spot prices are substantially below levels of 12 months ago. However, the spot market comprises only a small proportion of the total trade: by far the greater part of our alumina production is sold under long term contracts and here increased price levels have been evident. Aluminium prices on the free market are currently about 90 per cent of the world producer price, but again the proportion of aluminium sold on free markets is small. In the medium term, aluminium prices are expected to increase at above general inflation rates.
(6) My Department receives a representative selection of aluminium trade literature. In addition, my Department is able to supplement this information through bilateral trade relations and Australian membership of the International Bauxite Association, through the network of Departmental representatives overseas and through frequent contact with the Australian aluminium industry.

Re-alignment of the Cotter Road, Canberra
(Question No. 990)
Mr Porter asked the Minister for the Capital Territory, upon notice, on 1 April 1981 :

(1) Has there been a re-alignment of the Cotter Road adjacent to the Yarra Glen-Adelaide Avenue Overpass; if so, why.
(2) If the re-alignment relates to perceived safety aspects, how many accidents have there been on that road in the last 3 years and have there been any fatalities in these accidents.
(3) What was the estimated cost of the roadworks.
(4) What sum has been spent to date.
(5) When did the work start, when was it due for completion and when will it be completed.
Mr Hodgman-The answer to the honourable member's question is as follows:
I am advised by the National Capital Development Commission that:
(I) A re-alignment of Cotter Road adjacent to Dunrossil Drive and Dudley Street is being constructed at present. This involves the duplication of a short section of Cotter Road and reconstruction of the intersections with Dunrossil Drive and with Dudley Street.
The purpose of this work is to improve the operation of these intersections, by separating the turning traffic movements and providing median storage for these turning vehicles, which include official motorcades leaving from and returning to Government House at Yarralumla. This work will improve the safety of tfiese turning vehicles on a busy, high speed road.
(2) Accident records for 1980 are not yet available, but for the years 1977-1979 inclusive, the accident record for the section of road is:
1977-7 accidents (2 injuries) 1978-6 accidents (2 injuries) 1979-8 accidents (nil injuries)
There were no fatalities in this area during this period.
(3) The estimated cost of the works is $207,000.
(4) Progress payments totalling $85,000 have been made to 7 April 1981.
(5) Construction of roadworks commenced in December 1980 and is due for completion in May 1981. It is expected the work will be completed on schedule.

Women Veterans: Dependant's Allowance
(Question No. 1045)
Mr Milton asked the Minister representing the Minister for Veterans' Affairs, upon notice, on 2 Apri11981:
(1) Can a woman veteran, who has total care of her orphaned grandchild, and who is in receipt of a 10 per cent veterans' disability pension, claim the dependents allowance for these children, as is allowed for children or adopted children of the veteran; if not, why not.
(2) How is the dependents allowance of 28 cents per child per fortnight equated with a 10 per cent disability.
(3) Will the Minister consider extending this allowance to cover the orphaned grandchildren of veterans.
Mr Thomson-The Minister for Veterans' Affairs has provided the following answer to the honourable member's question:
(1) A veteran responsible for the care of orphaned grandchildren cannot claim the dependant's pension for such children. Repatriation legislation provides that only the children or adopted children of an incapacitated veteran are eligible to be granted children's dependant's pension.

3 bedroom house-I 5-20 months 4 bedroom house-16 months 2 bedroom flat-3t-16 months Bedsitter flat-7-8 months
(I) (b) Applicants for emergency housing fall into two separate categories. The true emergency group comprise of applicants who demonstrate an inability to accommodate themselves that night in any way whatsoever, ego because their home has been destroyed by fire. These few genuine emergency applicants can be accommodated on the same day. Other successful applicants for what is called emergency housing are those who can demonstrate that their circumstances are such that they would suffer undue hardship if required to wait out their time on the normal housing waiting period and therefore that an advance allocation ahead of all others on the normal waiting list is justified. These applicants, who are really applicants for priority housing, are generally housed within a period of four to six weeks.
(2) Apart from a general requirement that applicants for emergency housing must meet the eligibility criteria for entry to the normal waiting list for government rental housing, there are no set criteria which emergency housing applicants necessarily must meet. It is impossible to list all the circumstances in which applicants might qualify for assistance. However, the

Nomad Aircraft Program
(Question No. 1066)
Mr Hurford asked the Minister for Industry and Commerce, upon notice, on 7 April 1981:
(1) What is the present status of the Nomad aircraft program
(2) When will the current authorised production cease.
(3) Will the Government approve further production of the Nomad, in view of the deferral of the Tactical Fighter replacement decision.
(4) Are programs for the development of other aircraft types for production in Australia currently being undertaken; if so, what are the prospects for each of these types being brought into production.
Sir Phillip Lynch-The answer to the honourable member's question is as follows:
(1) On 15 December 1980 I announced that the Government had approved production of a further 55 Nomad aircraft, bringing the total number of aircraft authorised to date to 200. As at 30 April 1981, 128 Nomad aircraft had been ordered and of these 105 had been delivered.
(2) Production of the 200th Nomad aircraft is scheduled for completion in June 1984.
(3) Production authorisations beyond aircraft 200 will be considered by the Government at the appropriate time.
(4) There is a requirement to replace the RAAF basic pilot training aircraft (the CT-4 Airtrainer) and the advanced pilot training jet aircraft (the Macchi 326H) used by both RAN and RAAF. A Government-funded study by the Australian aircraft industry into the feasibility of designing and developing replacement aircraft locally has been completed. Principal recommendations are that local industry undertake the design, development, manufacture, sale and support of a basic trainer, and that for the advanced trainer, now required in a later time frame, discussions continue with selected overseas companies on the various possibilities for collaboration. The

project definition phase of the basic trainer project commenced in July 1980 and subject to satisfactory progress and further Government approvals the aircraft should enter production. Further studies are being carried out to examine possible collaborative programs for the advanced trainer aircraft.
Underspending of Estimates: Department of Finance
(Question No. HOl)
Mrs Darling asked the Minister representing the Minister for Finance, upon notice, on 7 April 1981:
(I) On what basis were the original 1980-81 appropriations made for division 304-Administrative.
(2) Has there been an underspending in this division by the estimate contained in the Department of Finance Information Paper, Statement of savings expected in annual appropriations made by Acts Nos 141 and 142-Appropriation Acts (Nos 1 and 2) 1980-81; if so, what is the reason.
Mr Howard-The Minister for Finance has provided the following answer to the honourable member's questions:
(1) The original 1980-81 appropriations made for my department for Division 304-Administrative, and for Divisions for other departments, were those presented to the Parliament in Appropriation Bill (No.1) 1980-81. The proposals, while in broad terms based on estimates submitted by departments, followed Government consideration of those estimates in the light of its policies in respect of particular areas of expenditure and of general financial and economic objectives.
The general principles applied to the preparation of estimates by departments and authorities are set out in the Directions issued by the Secretary of the Department of Finance under the authority of the Audit Act 1901. These Directions are long standing and have been endorsed by successive Governments. The relevant general principles are set out in Finance Direction 6.3 and provide in essence that:
(i) Estimates shall represent a realistic assessment of the sums expected to be spent and shall be based on current or known costs. Items of a recurring nature may be estimated on the basis of experience.
(ii) Estimates shall not include amounts in respect of proposals which are so far from firm that it is not possible to form any real estimate of what payment, if any, will be made.
(2) The Department of Finance Information Paper, 'Statement of Savings Expected in Annual Appropriations made by Acts Nos 141 and 142-Appropriation Acts (Nos 1 and 2) 1980-81', sets out amounts by which departments estimated, at the time of preparation of the Appropriation Bills (Nos 3 and 4) 1980-81, expenditure under the appropriation items concerned would at 30 June 1981 fall short of the appropriation originally made. What actual underspending will occur in respect of those or other items cannot be stated with certainty at this stage. The estimated savings would reflect for the most part the following circumstances:
(i) currcnt expectation that events would not occur by 30 June 1981, in cases where financial provisions had been made in the Appropriation Acts (Nos 1 and 2) 1980-81 in the then expectation that they would occur by that date;
(ii) compliance with Government policy that, as far as practicable, savings be offered as offsets to additional funds sought in the Appropriation Bills (Nos 3 and 4) 1980-81; and

Answers to Questions

13 May 1981 REPRESENTATIVES 2375

(iii) the results of efforts by departmental management to improve efficiency and thereby reduce costs.
Age Pension Means Test
(Question No. 1119)
Mrs Darling asked the Minister representing the Minister for Social Security, upon notice, on 7 April 1981:
(1) When was the age pension means test raised to $20 per week.
(2) Has the Minister's attention been drawn to a resolution passed at the Lilley area Liberal Party Conference on 15 March 1981, calling on the Commonwealth Government to raise the means test for age pensioners to $30 per week without loss of their pension.
(3) Does the Government plan to raise the means test before the Budget sittings; if not, why not.
(4) Will the Government consider this matter in preBudget deliberations.
Mr Hunt-The Minister for Social Security has provided the following answer to the honourable member's question:
(1) The amount of permissible private income ('free areas') before age pension is reduced was increased from $10 to $20 a week for a single person and from $17 to $34.50 for a married couple in September 1972.
(2) Yes.
(3) No. Possible changes to the age pension income test are regularly considered by the Government in the context of the annual Budget. In this way, they can be considered along with competing demands for increased spending and reduced taxation, against the background of the Government's overall economic strategy.
(4) The resolutions of the Lilley area Liberal Party Conference will be kept in mind by the Government during the 1981-82 pre-Budget deliberations.
South African Military Intervention in Angola
(Question No. 1153)
Mr Howe asked the Minister for Foreign Affairs, upon notice, on 8 April 1981 :
(1) Has his attention been drawn to the fact that the Reagan Administration is asking the Congress of the United States of America to lift the 1976 ban on aid to military and para-military operations in Angola.
(2) Has his attention also been drawn to the fact that one of the Angolan rebel leaders, Mr Jonas Savimbi, will visit .Washington D.C. shortly to meet United States officials.
(3) Will the USA by this action be seen to be collaborating militarily with South Africa, without whose aid Mr Savimbi's group could not survive.
(4) Does the Australian Government support South African military intervention in Angola.
Mr Street-The answers to the honourable member's questions are as follows:
(I) I have seen reports that the Administration is seeking to repeal the 1976 Clark Amendment which bans aid for the purpose of conducting military and para-military operations to groups or individuals in Angola except under certain precise conditions. The Administration has said that it seeks repeal on principle because the amendment is a restriction on executive authority.

(2) I have seen reports to this effect.
:,-'pr
(3) and (4) The United States Administration has said that its policy towards South Afrij:a is under review. President Reagan's nominee for the position of Under Secretary of State for African Affairs, Mr Chester Crocker, has recently visited a number of countries in Europe and Africa, including Angola, for discussions as part of that review. Until the results of the review have been announced, it is not possible to judge what effects any new United States policy might have, but the Government notes that in testimony to the United States Senate Foreign Relations Committee Mr Crocker said the United States Government did not intend to 'destabilize' the Government of Angola. The Australian Government recognises the MPLA as the Government of Angola. We have consistently deplored South African military intervention in Angola. South Africa's continued illegal occupation of Namibia has contributed dramatically to the heightening of tension along the border between Angola and Namibia. South Africa has mounted a number of raids into Angola allegedly against SWAPO positions which have caused direct contact between Angolan and South African armed forces. South African actions in Angola have been condemned by the United Nations Security Council which in June 1980 passed Resolution 465 (180) dealing with this question.
The Government regrets the suffering and loss of life which resulted from South African incursions. It considers the raids to be a violation of the principle of the mutual respect for the territorial integrity of all states. The Government also believes that the raids have contributed to the difficulties confronting the negotiations on the UNJWestern plan for a peaceful settlement to the issue of independence for Namibia.

Australian Cerebral Palsy Association
(Question No. 1188)
Mrs Darling asked the Minister representing the Minister for Social Security, upon notice, on 8 April 1981:
(1) Has the Minister received representations from or on behalf of the Australian Cerebral Palsy Association concerning initiatives which might be taken by the Government in the International Year of Disabled Persons.

2376 REPRESENTATIVES 13 May 1981

Answers to Questions

(2) If so, do those initiatives include removing the means test from the invalid pension, particularly as it relates to persons with cerebral palsy, in order to bring it into line with the means test free status to visually handicapped invalid pensioners.
(3) Have representations drawn attention to the unrealistic limit on earnings by disabled persons in sheltered employment.
(4) Is the invalid pension means test limit $20 and has the means test applied since 1972.
(5) If the means test had been indexed in relation to the increase in average wages, what would the means test limit now be.
(6) Will he consider raising the means test limit to that limit.
Mr Hunt-The Minister for Social Security has provided the following answer to the honourable member's question:
(1) and (2) Yes.
(3) Representations have drawn attention to the limit on earnings for people employed in sheltered workshops.
(4) The maximum rate of invalid pension is payable to a single person without dependants if weekly income does not exceed $20. In the case of a married couple or where there are dependants the amount is higher. This figure has applied since the income test was introduced in November 1976. Prior to November 1976 a means test applied which took into account income and a notional value of property. Between October 1972 and November 1976 the limit of income and notional value of property for the payment of a maximum rate of invalid pension was $20.
(5) If the figure of $20 was increased by the movement in seasonally adjusted average weekly earnings between the December quarter 1972 and the December quarter 1980 (the latest available), the new figure would be $56.50.
(6) Possible changes to the invalid pension income test are regularly considered by the Government in the context of the annual Budget. In this way it can then be considered along with competing demands for increased spending and reduced taxation, against the background of the Government's overall economic strategy.

Age Pensions: Means Test
(Question No. 1674)
Mr Bungey asked the Minister representing the Minister for Social Security, upon notice, on 9 April 1981:
What would be the cost to the Commonwealth Government in a financial year if aged pensioners were permitted to earn (a) $25 and (b) $30 per week before affecting their pension payments.
Mr Hunt The Minister for Social Security has provided the following answer to the honourable member's question:
The estimated full year cost to the Government of increasing thc income that age pensioners are permitted to receive before affecting their pension entitlement is:
(a) some $30111 if the free area of income is increased by $5 a week, i.e. to $25 a week for single pensioners and to $22.25 a week for each partner in the case of pensioner couples.

(b) some $60m if the free area of income is increased by $10 a week, i.e. to $30 a week for single pensioners and to $27.25 a week for each partner in the case of pensioner couples.
These costs are based on rates of pension, and numbers of pensioners current, at December 1980, and are gross costs in that they make no allowance for the additional revenue which may result from the taxation of the payments concerned.
Fire Extinguishers
(Question No. 1741)
Mr Bungey asked the Minister for Science and Technology upon notice, on 9 April 1981 :
What action has been taken by his Department concerning the finding by the Department of Housing and Construction reported in the 1979-80 Annual Report of the Commonwealth Fire Board, that sci f-contained automatic halon gas fire extinguishers installed in fume cupboards where acid chemicals are used deteriorate rapidly.
Mr Thomson-The answer to the honourable member's question is as follows:
My Department is aware of the Department of Housing and Construction advice to the Commonwealth Fire Board as detailed in the Commonwealth Fire Board 1979-80 Annual Report. My Department is advised by the Department of Housing and Construction in the design and installation of fire extinguishing systems for its laboratories.
In laboratories within the Department's Analytical Services Branch the only installation where halon gas cylinders are located within fume cupboards is in a laboratory specifically designed for pesticide residue analysis in which no acids are used.
Hazardous Chemicals
(Question No. 1751)
Mr Bungey asked the Minister for Administrative Services, upon notice, on 9 April 1981 :
What progress has been made in preparing a national system of coding hazardous chemicals used for fire fighting purposes.
Mr Newman-The answer to the honourable member's question is as follows:
The Commonwealth Fire Board has advised that on 4 July 1980, the Australian Transport Advisory Council endorsed the Australian Code for the Transport of Dangerous Goods by Road and Rail. This incorporated the UK Hazchem Code system which provides for a placard display externally on all vehicles carrying dangerous goods, giving information to emergency services on the immediate actions to be taken in the event of a mishap.
State and Commonwealth Governments have agreed to implement the new Code on a uniform basis throughout Australia as a matter of priority and each Government has the responsibility for bringing this about in the manner it considers appropriate.
These measures apply of course to hazardous chemicals generally and not only those used for Hre fighting purposes.
Hazardous Chemicals
(Question No. 1752)
Mr Bungey asked the Minister for Administrative Services, upon notice, on 9 April 1981:

2380 REPRESENTATIVES 13 May 1981

Answers to Questions

What action has been taken to prepare uniform and properly publicised safety practices for hazardous chemicals in Australia.
Mr Newman-The answer to the honourable member's question is as follows:
The Commonwealth Fire Board has advised that uniform safety practices for hazardous chemicals are now covered by the new Australian Code for the Transport of Dangerous Goods by Road and Rail which States and Commonwealth Governments have agreed to implement. It is now a matter for each Government to arrange this as it considers appropriate.
In the meantime, the Australian Chemical Industry Council has recommended to its members the voluntary placarding of vehicles with the Code's requirements on emergency information.
The Standards Association of Australia is also producing cards of emergency procedure guides for a number of chemicals for carrying in cabins of transport vehicles as a guide to drivers in the event of emergencies involving fire or escape of cargo.
Other safety practices for hazardous chemicals are covered in existing or proposed Occupational Safety and 'Health in Commonwealth Government Employment Codes of Practice prepared by the former Department of Productivity (now the Department of Science and, Technology).
Fire Extinguishers
(Question No. 1753)
Mr Bungey asked the Minister for Administrative Service, upon notice, on 9 April 1981:
(l) How many incidents of faulty or defective fire extinguishers were reported to the Commonwealth Fire Board in 1979-80.
(2) What type of extinguishers were they, and what action has been taken on the reports.
Mr Newman-The answer to the honourable member's question is as follows:
(1) Two.
(2) The Commonwealth Fire Board has advised that the two types of extinguishers involed were Halogenated Hydrocarbon type (Halon 1301) and Water (soda acid) type. In both instances the defects were brought to the attention of the manufacturers and the Standards Association of Australia.
In the case of the Halogenated Hydrocarbon extinguishers which were of the stored pressure, modular type unit, the manufacturer has indicated that the faulty units have been recalled and the fault eliminated. Departments and authorities were advised of the defects and as a safety measure, were requested to ensure that any such units held were checked.
Defects found in the Water (soda acid) extinguisher related to the failure of plastic caps. Pressure testing of extinguishers has been recommended and also the Board will be recommending that the failed caps be replaced with metal type caps.
Australian Federal Police Force: Use ofDrug Addicted Dogs

Mr Newman-The answer to the honourable member's question is as follows:
(l) 15 German Shepherd dogs.
(2) Guarding, explosives detection, and general police work.
(3) I am advised that in accordance with established practice and consistent with the recommendations of the Australian Royal Commission of Inquiry into Drugs, the Bureau of Customs provides the services of drug detector dogs to the Australian Federal Police when requested. I am further advised that the dogs are not drug addicted.
(4) Salaries applicable to the members concerned plus a dog handler's allowance of 43¢ per shift.
Passports: Prosecutions
(Question No. 1758)
Mr Bungey asked the Minister for Administrative Services, upon notice, on 9 April 1981:
How many prosecutions were launched by the Australian Federal Police in each of the last three years for false or amended (a) Australian and (b) foreign passports.
Mr Newman-The answer to the honourable member's question is as follows:
I have been informed by the Commissioner of the Australian Federal Police that passport infringement records have only been maintained by the Australian Federal Police since July 1979 and do not differentiate between Australian and foreign passports.
In the period July 1979 to June 1980 ten prosecutions were launched relating to forged and amended passports. From July 1980 until the present time a further ten prosecutions have been launched.
These figures only relate to prosecutions launched under the Passport Act 1938. Other prosecutions would have been commenced under the Crimes Act 1914 but not statistical breakdown is available.

Applied Ecology Pty Ltd: Turtle Seminar
(Question No. 1865)
Mr Bungey asked the Minister for Home Affairs and Environment, upon notice, on 9 April 1981:
What action has the Government taken on the concern expressed at the Turtle Seminar conducted by Applied Ecology Pty Ltd in conjunction with the James Cook University in June 1979 at the large number of female turtles being harvested in the Torres Strait/Gulf of Papua area and the effects this harvesting is having on the wild turtle popUlation of the area.
Mr Wilson-The answer to the honourable member's question is as follows:
Internationally, marine turtles are considered to be threatened with extinction largely as a result of past uncontrolled commercial exploitation. Australian territorial waters contain some of the largest populations of marine turtles remaining in the world and the Government acknowledges its responsibility for ensuring these endangered species are adequately protected and managed in such a manner that will assure their long-term conservation.
The Third Meeting of Parties to the Convention on International Trade in Endangered Species of Wild Fauna and Flora (CITES) held recently in New Delhi, considered and adopted Australian proposals to include two Australian species of marine turtle on Appendix I to the Convention. This has resulted in all marine turtles being listed on Appendix I to the Convention thereby prohibiting international commercial trade in products derived from them.

Torres Strait Islanders have traditionally hunted marine turtles for food and subsistence. The Torres Strait between Australia and Papua New Guinea, while protecting the traditional way of life, including traditional fishing, also allows for the adoption of conservation measures in respect of endangered or threatened wildlife. The Government is concerned to ensure that its obligations under this Treaty and CITES are honoured.
Following the closure of turtle farming activities in Torres Strait by Applied Ecology Pty Ltd, the Australian National Parks and Wildlife Service engaged the Queensland National Parks and Wildlife Service to undertake a pilot study. The objectives of this study are:
to determine the need. for further detailed research on marine turtles in the region; and
to determine the impact of subsistence harvesting on marine turtle numbers.
The report of this preliminary study is expected early in the 1981-82 financial year.
Telephone Calls by Staff: Department of the Prime Minister and Cabinet
(Question No. 1867)
Mr Bungey asked the Prime Minister, upon notice, on 9 April 1981:
(1) Has his attention been drawn to a report in the West Australian of 18 July 1979, page 37, that London metropolitan police reduced its telephone bill by $985,000, or about 20 per cent, by use of a monitoring device which recorded length, time and destination of telephone calls and the extension from which the call was made.
(2) Are personal telephone calls, particularly trunk and STD calls, made by staff of his Department controlled; if so, in what way.
(3) Has any consideration been given to the introduction of monitoring devices for telephone calls in his Department; if so, what was the outcome of the consideration.
Mr Malcolm Fraser-The answer to the honourable member's question is as follows:
(1) Yes.
(2) and (3) Except for those to senior officers, extensions to the Departmental switchboard are barred from access to the STD facility. Official trunk or STD calls must be approved by a senior officer. Personal trunk calls must be booked through the switchboard operator and recovery action taken.

Sickness Benefit
(Question No. 1900)
Mr Humphreys asked the Minister representing the Minister for Social Security, upon notice, on 9 April 1981:
Are eligible applicants for sickness benefit who are in part-time employment as juniors and who are receiving substantially less than the usual sickness benefit paid weekly to single under 18 year olds, entitled to the full basic rate for that category, of $36 per week.
Mr Hunt-The Minister for Social Security has provided the following answer to the honourable member's question:
No. The Social Services Act provides that the weekly rate of sickness benefit payable to a person cannot exceed the weekly rate of salary, wages or other income which that person has lost by reason of his incapacity. This means that if a claimant's loss of income is less than the maximum amount of sickness benefit, the rate of sickness benefit payable is limited to the claimant's actual loss of income.
Superannuation
Mr Howard-On 31 March 1981 (Hansard, page 1087-8) Mr Tambling asked me, as Minister representing the Minister for Finance, a question, without notice, concerning superannuation coverage for the Northern Territory Government's permanent part-time employment scheme.
The Minister for Finance has provided the following information in answer to the honourable member's question.
The introduction of permanent part-time employment in the Australian Public Service and other areas of Commonwealth employment is currently under consideration by the Public Service Board and other employing authorities such as Telecom Australia.
The Department of Finance has been examining the superannuation arrangements that are capable of being provided to permanent part-time employees should the Government decide to extend superannuation cover to them.
Northern Territory employees comprise a relatively small proportion of the number of contributors to the Commonwealth Superannuation Scheme. It would not be appropriate to consider extending cover under the Superannuation Act to permanent part-time employees of the Northern Territory before decisions have been taken on the terms and conditions to apply to permanent part-time employment in the Commonwealth generally. Such terms and conditions are primarily the responsibility of the employing authorities.

I understand the matter may take some time to resolve but arrangements have been made to keep the Chief Minister informed of progress.
Health Care Services
Mr MacKellar-On 30 April 1981 (Hansard, page 1807) Mr Cunningham asked me a question, without notice, concerning hospital insurance cover for confinements.
The answer to the honourable member's question is as follows:
Health funds generally have waiting periods not exceeding two months before basic medical and basic hospital benefits are paid to a new contributor. Basic medical benefits currently cover at least 75% of the Schedule medical fees with a maximum patient contribution of $10 where the Schedule fee is charged. From 1 September 1981 the basic table will cover 85% of the Schedule fees (with $10 maximum patient contribution). Basic hospital benefits provide full cover to the equivalent of the cost of shared ward accommodation in a public hospital (currently $50 a day). From I September, while the charges may increase, full cover for shared ward accommodation will remain.
Other exclusions from benefits such as for pre-existing conditions are not permitted for basic levels of benefits. This means that persons joining a health fund now, including obstetric cases, would be entitled to basic benefits after two months.
Because of the new arrangements to be introduced from I September 1981, and the changed circumstances that will apply to many people, health funds will be required to waive the waiting period for basic benefits for new members joining between I September and 31 October 1981. This is to allow people adequate time to make insurance arrangements so as not to be disadvantaged because of waiting periods that would otherwise apply.
People seeking higher insurance cover e.g. 100% medical and higher levels of hospital accommodation, generally are subject to further exclusions on the additional benefits (nine months waiting periods for obstetric cases, exclusions for preexisting conditions, maximum benefits etc).
These restrictions will not be subject to waiver between 1 September and 31 October 1981.
Fortescue Oil Field
Mr Malcolm Fraser-On 24 March 1981 (Hansard, page 812) Mr Keating asked me a question, without notice, concerning royalties for Bass Strait oil.
The following is in answer to the honourable member's question:
In accordance with the offshore petroleum legislation, royalties are assessed on the wellhead value which is determined by deducting from the selling price certain costs involved in the processing and transportation of the oil from the offshore platform to the selling point. Payments of crude oil excise are also deducted. Where crude oil excise is not payable, the wellhead value will be higher and royalty payments accordingly greater. The legislation provides for basic royalties to be shared 40 per cent to the Commonwealth and 60 per cent to the States.
The current arrangments for determining wellhead value in regard to Bass Strait production were agreed by the Commonwealth and Victoria. This joint approach will continue under

Answers to Questions

13 May 1981 REPRESENTATIVES 2387

the revised offshore petroleum arrangements when they become effective under the offshore constitutional settlement, but in the event of any irreconcilable difference the Commonwealth will have the final say. The current royalty sharing arrangements are to continue under the new legislative arrangments.
Coastal Surveillance: Expenditure by Department of Transport
(Question No. 566)
Mr Morrison asked the Minister for Transport, upon notice, on 24 February 1981:
(1) What sum was (a) appropriated and (b) actually spent by his Department on coastal surveillance for (i) 1977-78, (ii) 1978-79 and (iii) 1979-80.
(2) What were the main items of expenditure and the cost attributed to each item.
Mr Hunt-The answer to the honourable member's question is as follows:
(1) (a) During the period 1977-78, 1978-79 no specific sum was appropriated for coastal surveillance. Funds were included in appropriations to cover the total operations of my Department. In 1979-80 an amount of $1.5m was appropriated under Division 655/2/21 to finance the cost of evaluating Nomad aircraft for a role in coastal surveillance. No other funds were specifically appropriated.
(b) The amounts actually spent on coastal surveillance are reflected in various items of Divisions 655/1 and 655/2 e.g. salaries, overtime, communications, etc. No expenditure was incurred under Division 655/2/21.
(2) Except for Division 655/2/21 expenditure under each item attributable to coastal surveillance has not been determined because of the relatively minor nature of the coastal surveillance program compared with other Departmental activities. However an assessment of costs is being undertaken as part of the Government review of the coastal surveillance program.
Department of Transport: Functions
(Question No. 596)
Mr McLean asked the Minister for Transport, upon notice, on 25 February 1981:
What are the functions of the (a) Policy and Planning and (b) Surface Transport Operations areas of his Department.
Mr Hunt-The answer to the honourable member's question is as following:
(a) The functions of the Policy and Planning area of my Department are:
Development of policies on the most effective allocation of resources among the various transport modes .
Encouragement and promotion of continuous socially acceptable improvement, innovation, growth and phase-out of modal and inter-modal commercial and personal transport
Assistance in the formulation of policy for transport undertakings which are authorities of the Commonwealth Government or in which the Commonwealth is a shareholder
Planning, direction and co-ordination of total departmental contribution to specified major transport projects, e.g. Major Airport Needs of Sydney (MANS) Study
Provision of the Secretariats for the Australian Transport Advisory Council, Aviation Industry Advisory Council, Marine and Ports Council of Australia, and Transport Industries Advisory Council

Development of Policies in association with other Departments where relevant, on Australia's involvement in international organisations and multilateral agreements concerned with transport.
Rationalisation and licensing of air transport services Approval of fares, freight rates and timetables for airline operations and administration of financial arrangements in relation to airlines Issue of approvals for export of aircraft and permits for import of aircraft and administration of aircraft acquisition policies
Negotiation and administration of international air transport agreements and administration of the regulation of international freights and services
Formulation of policy in regard to overseas and coastal shipping, including the licensing of vessels to trade on the Australian coast
Ensuring the provision of adequate shipping services to meet the needs of the export trade
Development of policy on national aspects of development of ports and ship repair facilities
Collection of stevedoring and related port statistics Administration and development of policy in relation to the Tasmanian Freight Equalisation Scheme Development of policies for the provision of financial assistance for road construction and maintainance for transport planning and research Development of policies in relation to financial assistance to State railway systems, rail standardisation and developmental rail projects; and Administration of grants to the States for the improvement of urban transport systems in capital cities. (b) The functions of the Surface Operations area of my Department are:
Provision, administration, operation and maintenance of marine navigational aids
Survey and inspection of Australian and foreign ships for compliance with Commonwealth marine legislation and international conventions on safety and pollution
Investigation of shipping casualties and oversight of general navigation safety matters
Administration of the Register of Australian Ships Oversight of the supply of seamen, manning of Australian ships and the qualifications of sea-going personnel Development of maritime communications and traffic operation systems Provision of operational co-ordination of coastal surveillance activities and servicing of the Australian coastal surveillance organisation Provision of a marine search and rescue service including a ship position reporting system Development and administration of policy relating to marine pollution and oversight of the functioning of the National Plan to Combat Pollution of the Sea by Oil Conduct of research into road safety, the development of vehicle safety, emission standards and uniform traffic codes Provision of advice to the Australian Motor Vehicle Certification Board on the compliance of vehicles with the Australian Design Rules
Establishment of policies, formulation of standards, conduct of research and development of educational programs related to road safety
Administration of road legislation providing funds to the States for construction and maintenance
Evaluation of State road proposals and maintenance of continual liaison with State road authorities on plans and proposals for roads other than National Roads
In co-operation with the States development of priorities and implementation of plans for National Roads; and
Co-ordination of the development of standards for, and studies into the location of the National Highway System

Disaster Relief
(Question No. 666)
Mrs Darling asked the Prime Minister, ilpon notice, on 3 March 1981:
(1) Has his attention been drawn to a State Emergency Service report on the damage resulting from a severe hailstorm which struck the North Brisbane bayside suburbs of Brighton and Sandgate on 16 December 1980.
(2) If so, did the report describe the storm as Queensland's worst natural disaster to that date since the formation of the State Emergency Service in 1975.
(3) Was any request received by him from the Queensland Premier for assistance from the Commonwealth Government for relief aid following that hailstorm'.
(4) Was it necessary for the Queensland Premier to declare the area adversely affected by the storm a disaster area, before

the Commonwealth Government would provide special assistance.
(5) With respect to the Commonwealth Government's undertaking to meet on a dollar for dollar basis State expenditures on standard measures for the immediate relief of personal hardship and distress, as referred to on page 79 of Budget Paper No.7, 1980-81, (a) what are regarded as standard measures, (b) must the State Government request assistance before it is given, (c) has assistance been provided to the Queensland State Government for (i) the Brighton hailstorm relief, (ii) North Queensland flood relief and (iii) Dalby flood relief.
(6) How many declared natural disasters have occurred in each State and Territory in each year since 1940, and in each case what sum has the Commonwealth contributed by way of disaster relief assistance.
(7) Is it a fact that Queensland suffers more from natural disasters such as drought, cyclones and floods, than any other State in the Commonwealth.
(8) While campaigning in the Electoral Division of McPherson, or at any other time, has he been informed of the Queensland Premier's recently expressed intention to request amendments to the disaster relief arrangements between the Commonwealth and State Governments.
(9) Has his attention been drawn to the report in the Courier Mail, 16 February 1981, in which the Queensland Premier is quoted as saying that the present disaster 'idief arrangements with the Federal Government do not cover disasters like the Brighton storm; if so, is there any substance to this claim.
(10) Is it a fact that once a State Government passes its prescribed base expenditure for natural disasters before the end of a financial year, the Commonwealth Government is obliged to contribute toward the total cost of disaster relief.
(11) Is it also a fact that when a State Government expends in relief aid on anyone disaster in excess of one tenth of its prescribed base expenditure for natural disasters, the Commonwealth is obliged to contribute towards relief measures on a $3 for $1 basis.
(12) How does the Government determine whether grants for relief of personal hardship and distress come within the conditions of the existing disaster arrangements and thereby qualify for Commonwealth reimbursement.
Mr Malcolm Fraser-The answer to the honourable member's question is as follows:
(1) and (2) In the days immediately following the storm, the Commonwealth received, through the Natural Disasters Organisation, regular situation reports from the Queensland State Emergency Service on the damage and the emergency relief operations, along with requests for Defence Force equipment and personnel to assist in the relief operations. Defence Force assistance was provided as requested. Other State Emergency Service reports are prepared for and used by the State and are not necessarily forwarded to the Commonwealth.
(3) Yes. Commonwealth assistance was, in any case, available to the State under existing natural disaster relief arrangements for a range of measures. Those applicable to the Brighton hailstorm and other similar occurrances were for relief of personal hardship and distress and for the restoration of public assets and emergency protection and repair work by State, local and semi-government authorities. The Queensland Premier advised the Commonwealth on 22 December 1980 of the implementation of these measures and sought agreement, for the purpose of determining Commonwealth assistance to the State under the arrangements, to variation of limits applicable in individual cases to certain of the

Answers to Questions

13 May 1981 REPRESENTATIVES 2389

measures for relief of personal hardship and distress.l replied in January 1981 indicating the Commonwealth's agreement to the new limits proposed.
(4) The declaration of an area as a disaster area is solely a matter for the State Government. Commonwealth assistance for natural disaster relief is not contingent on a State making such a declaration.
(5) (a) Standard measures for the relief of personal hardship and distress under the natural disaster relief arrangements comprise:
(i) provision of emergency food (ii) provision of emergency clothing (iii) provision of emergency accommodation (iv) essential repairs to houses to make them habitable and
secure (v) repair and replacement of essential items of furniture
and personal effects
These measures are restricted to people in needy circumstances as a result of a disaster.
(5) (b) Not in respect of the standard measures.
(5) (c) (i), (ii) and (iii) Assistance has been provided in respect of claims made by the State Government in relation to these and other disasters.
(6) The natural disaster relief arrangements are not designed to provide assistance to a State on a disaster-by-disaster basis, but rather to assist a State in meeting the cost of natural disaster relief assistance provided during a year when it goes beyond the amount originally set to rellect the State's financial capacity. Accordingly, information in the form sought is not readily available. Details of Commonwealth assistance to individual States under the natural disaster relief arrangements are available in 1980-81 Budget Paper No.7 (pp 79-80, 139-141) and in earlier issues of this Budget Paper.
(7) It is not possible to assess from the information available to the Commonwealth whether Queensland suffers more from natural disasters than any other State. Such an assessment would depend not only on the frequency of disasters but also on the extent of damage, both to property and individuals, caused by disasters, not all of which would attract Commonwealth assistance.
(8) When the present arrangements were established in 1978, provision was made for regular reviews. On 4 March 1981 I announced that I had written to all State Premiers and the Chief Minister of the Northern Territory proposing that officials consult on a review of the current arrangements to enable consideration at the June 1981 Premiers' Conference. Prior to the announcement of the review, several Premiers, including Mr Bjelke-Petersen, had written to me indicating dissatisfaction with aspects of the arrangements as they affected their States.
(9) As indicated in (5) above, the Brighton hailstorm has already attracted Commonwealth assistance in respect of the immediate relief of personal hardship and distress. Expenditure by Queensland on longer-term relief and restoration measures that is in accord with the terms and conditions of the natural disaster relief arrangements would be eligible to attract Commonwealth assistance. Additionally, as was indicated in (1) and (2) above, Defence Force assistance was provided immediately following the storm to meet urgent State requests for manpower and equipment for the emergency relief and restoration operations.
(10) and (11) The Commonwealth contributes, on a $3 for $1 basis, to the cost of State expenditures on approved relief and restoration measures (including measures for the relief of personal hardship and distress) in excess of the State's base amount where such expenditures are in respect of a 'major' disaster, A 'major' disaster is, for this purpose, defined as a disaster in respect of which State expenditure on approved

measures exceeds one-tenth of the State's base amount. Where a disaster is not a 'major' disaster and/or the State has not exceeded its base amount, State expenditures on approved measures for the relief of personal hardship and distress attract Commonwealth assistance on a $ for $ basis. Further information can be found in 1980-81 Budget Paper No.7 (pages 79-80).
(12) The conditions under which Commonwealth assistance in respect of a State's expenditure on the relief of personal hardship and distress is provided, are described in (5) and (11) above. The States are required to submit audited statements to certify expenditure in accordance with agreed measures of assistance for natural disaster relief and restoration.
Uranium Resources at Nabarlek
(Question No. 780)
Mr Uren asked the Minister for Trade and Resources, upon notice, on 11 March 1981:
Further to his statement of 24 November 1980 made to the Sydney Chamber of Commerce (Hansard, 26 November 1980, page 55) that on the basis of the estimated resources at Nabadek, Northern Territory about 60 per cent of those resources have already been committed under contract or firm letters of intent, (a) what are the estimated uranium resources at Nabadek, (b) to which countries have these commitments been made, (c) in each case what is the volume of uranium committed under (i) contract and (ii) firm letters of intent, (d) in each case what approvals have been granted by the Government and (e) in each case what safeguard arrangements apply to these commitments.
Mr Anthony-I refer the honourable member to my reply to his earlier Question on Notice No. 275 (House of Representatives Hansard, 27 April 1981, pages 1683-1684).
Taxation Levels
(Question No. 799)
Mr Willis asked the Prime Minister, upon notice, on 12 March 1981:
What are the titles and full references of the many expert inquiries that have believed that the highest level of tax that ought to be payable by any person is about 50 cents in the dollar to which he referred in the House on 26 February 1981 (Hansard, page 235).
Mr Malcolm Fraser-The answer to the honourable member's question is as follows:
The Taxation Review Committee (1975) stated in its full report (Page 192) that a reduction in the maximum marginal rate to the region of 50 per cent would be an appropriate long term target.
The Canadian Royal Commission on Taxation (1967) stated in its final recommendations (Para. 315) that the maximum marginal tax rate should be held down to 50 per cent.
The United States Treasury report, 'Blue Prints for Basic Tax Reform' (1977) proposed two model tax systems as the basis for future tax reform-both of which involved reducing the maximum marginal rate below 50 per cent.
Bushfires: Use of Water-bombers
(Question No. 814)
Mr Les Johnson asked the Minister for Transport, upon notice, on 12 March 1981:

2390 REPRESENTATIVES 13 May 1981

Answers to Questions

(I) Has the Australian Government received a firm offer of sale of aircraft designed to be used as water-bombers against bushfires; if so, (a) when was the otTer made, (b) what was the name of the company making the offer and (c) what was the proposed contract price.
(2) Has he made a response to this offer; if so, what are the details.
Mr Hunt-The answer to the honourable member's question is as follows:
(I) (a) An offer was made to sell to the Australian Government two CL21S Water Bomber Aircraft with spares and ground support equipment.
(b) The offer was set out in a letter addressed to me dated 4 November 1980 from a Mr J. A. Nicas of Canadair Services Ltd, Montreal, Canada, the manufacturer of the aircraft.

(c) The total price in Canadian dollars for the package consisting of both aircraft, spares and equipment, was S11 ,840,000.
(2) In my letter ofreply to Mr Nicas I mentioned that the offer had been referred to my colleague, the Minister for Science and Technology for his consideration. Canadair Services Ltd were aware of the Prime Minister's press release of 22 September 1980 announcing that the Commonwealth Scientific and Industrial Research Organisation was to conduct a study to evaluate the effectiveness of using aircraft to fight forest and bush fires.
I also pointed out that my Department had no expertise in bushfire fighting. Its involvement in the use of civil aircraft for this purpose would primarily be concerned with the prescription of operational safety requirements and airworthiness matters.
Since referring the matter to my colleague the Minister for Science and Technology, no further approaches have been made to my Department concerning the sale of these aircraft.
